Distinguishing Rational and Natural Indication
Author classifies indications into three types and explains rational and natural with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given indication as rational or natural by testing whether the connection between the sign (malzum) and the signified (lazim) is logically necessary or merely habitual.

Core Idea
Rational indication is logically necessary; natural indication is based on Allah's habitual system.

This passage introduces the foundational classification of indications in Islamic logic. Understanding the difference between rational and natural indication is crucial for evaluating how signs lead to certain knowledge in the Islamic sciences, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the malzum and lazim
For any given indication, first identify what is indicating (malzum) and what is indicated (lazim). For example, in 'smoke indicates fire', smoke is the malzum and fire is the lazim.
The malzum is the sign, the lazim is what it points to.
2
Test for logical necessity
Ask: can the malzum exist without the lazim in the abstract mind? If the mind cannot conceive the malzum without the lazim, the connection is rational and logically necessary. If the mind can conceive separation, the connection is not rational.
Rational indication does not require empirical observation; it is a priori.
3
Check if the connection is based on natural habit
If the mind can conceive separation but repeated observation shows a consistent correlation, the indication is natural. It depends on Allah's customary way (sunnah) in creation.
Natural indication is learned through experience and observation.
4
Classify the indication
If logically necessary, it is rational indication (dalala 'aqliyya). If only habitual, it is natural indication (dalala tabi'iyya). If based on convention, it would be conventional, but that is not covered here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدّلالات وأقسَامُها دلالة شيء على شيء آخر لا بد أن تكون واحدة من الدلالات الثلاث التالية : أولاً : فهي إما أن تكون دلالة عقلية بحتة، كدلالة الأثر على المؤثر، وكدلالة الحركة بالإرادة على وجود الحياة، وكدلالة حركة اليد على حركة الخاتم الموجود في إصبع من أصابعها، وكدلالة مسير السفينة في البحر على تحرك ركابها وفق حركتها، وهكذا كل ملزوم إذ يدلُّ على لازمه العقلي البحت . ثانياً : وإما أن تكون دلالة طبيعية، وهي الدلالة التي ليس بين الملزوم واللازم فيها ارتباط عقلي، إلا أن النظام الذي وضعه الله في الطبيعة قد أوجد هذا الترابط، فإذا سألنا العقل المجرد عن ملاحظة النظام الموجود في الطبيعة لم يجد تعليلاً عقلياً له؛ غير أن الاختبار المتكرر للأحداث الطبيعية قد نبه على وجود هذا الترابط في الواقع، ولكن ليس لدى العقل المجرد مانع من انفكاكه لو ثبت ذلك في الواقع ولو نادراً . مثال ذلك : دلالة ارتفاع درجة حرارة جسم الإنسان على حالةٍ من حالات المرض، ودلالة التقيؤ والإسهال الشديدين على الإصابة بمرض الهيضة (الكوليرا)، ودلالة حمرة الوجه على حالة الخجل في النفس، ودلالة صفرة الوجه على حالة الوجل في النفس، ودلالة الكلام على حياة الإنسان
Indications and their divisions: The indication of one thing for another must be one of the following three types: First: it is either a purely rational indication, like the indication of the effect for the cause, and like the indication of voluntary motion for the existence of life, and like the indication of the movement of the hand for the movement of the ring on one of its fingers, and like the indication of the movement of a ship in the sea for the movement of its passengers in accordance with its motion, and so on every necessary concomitant (malzum) indicates its purely rational necessary property (lazim). Second: and it is a natural indication, which is the indication in which there is no rational connection between the referent (malzum) and the referent property (lazim), except that the system which Allah has placed in nature has produced this correlation. So if we ask the abstract mind to observe the system present in nature, it does not find a rational explanation for it; however, repeated testing of natural events has alerted to the existence of this correlation in reality, but the abstract mind finds no impediment to its separation even if that were to occur in reality, even rarely. An example of that: the indication of an elevated body temperature for a state of illness, and the indication of severe vomiting and diarrhea for infection with the disease of cholera, and the indication of redness of the face for a state of shyness in the psyche, and the indication of paleness of the face for a state of fear in the psyche, and the indication of speech for the life of a human.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of indication (dalala). The author states that any indication of one thing for another must be one of three types. However, only the first two are detailed in the provided text. The first type is rational indication (dalala 'aqliyya), where the connection between the indicating thing (malzum) and the indicated thing (lazim) is purely rational and necessary. Examples given include the effect indicating the cause, voluntary motion indicating life, the movement of a hand indicating the movement of a ring on it, and the movement of a ship indicating the movement of its passengers. In each case, the relationship is logically necessary. The second type is natural indication (dalala tabi'iyya), where there is no rational connection but rather a natural system established by Allah. The mind sees no logical necessity, but repeated observation reveals a correlation. Examples include fever indicating illness, vomiting and diarrhea indicating cholera, facial redness indicating shyness, paleness indicating fear, and speech indicating life. The author notes that the abstract mind does not find it impossible for these to be disconnected, even if rarely. The third type, conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya), is not discussed in this excerpt. This classification is foundational in Islamic logic for understanding how signs lead to knowledge.

B) دلالة الأثر على المؤثر
C) دلالة حمرة الوجه على الخجل
D) دلالة الكلام على الحياة

Key Takeaway
Indication is either rational (logically necessary) or natural (habitual from Allah's system), and distinguishing them is key to sound reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Three Types of Verbal Conventional Indication
Enumeration and definition of three types of verbal conventional indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of verbal conventional indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and correctly classify examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Words indicate meaning in three ways: fully, partially, or by necessary association.

Understanding how words convey meaning is essential for precise thinking in the Islamic sciences. This lesson introduces the three types of verbal conventional indication according to the logicians: complete indication (mutabaqa), partial indication (tadammun), and concomitant indication (iltizam). Mastering these categories will sharpen your ability to analyze linguistic evidence in usul al-fiqh, tafsir, and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division basis
The passage divides indication based on whether the word signals its complete meaning, part of it, or an external necessary concomitant. Notice the three 'إما' clauses that structure the division.
2
Define mutabaqa (مطابقة)
This is when the word indicates its entire real (or metaphorical) meaning. Example: the word 'human' indicating the whole essence of humanity. Identify it as the default, straightforward indication.
3
Define tadammun (تضمن)
This is when the word indicates a part of its real (or metaphorical) meaning. Example: 'human' indicating 'animal' or 'rational'. Note that the part is actually contained in the definition.
4
Define iltizam (التزام)
This is when the word indicates something external to its meaning but necessary for it rationally or conventionally. Example: 'roof' indicating 'wall' (rational necessity: a roof cannot exist without a wall). The indicated meaning is not part of the word's definition.
5
Distinguish tadammun from iltizam
Tadammun indicates a part of the actual meaning; iltizam indicates something outside the meaning but connected. Practice by testing whether the indicated item is part of the definition (tadammun) or a necessary external condition (iltizam).
This is the most common point of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية : تنقسم الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية إلى ثلاثة أقسام كما ذكرنا : وذلك لأن الكلام إما أن يُساق ليدل على تمام معناه، وإما أن يُساق ليدل على بعض معناه، وإما أن يُساق ليدل على معنى آخر خارج عن معناه، إلا أنه لازم له عقلاً أو عرفاً، فهذه هي الوجوه التي تشتمل على الأقسام الثلاثة للدلالة الوضعية اللفظية، ولكل قسم منها اسم اصطلاحي : أ ـ فدلالة اللفظ على تمام معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي (مطابقة) . ب ـ ودلالة اللفظ على بعض معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي (تضمن) . جـ ـ ودلالة اللفظ على معنى آخر خارج عن معناه لازم له عقلاً أو عرفاً (التزام) .
Sections of verbal conventional indication: The verbal conventional indication is divided into three sections as we mentioned: That is because speech is either brought to indicate its complete meaning, or to indicate part of its meaning, or to indicate another meaning external to its meaning, but it is necessary for it rationally or conventionally. These are the aspects that contain the three sections of verbal conventional indication, and each section has a technical name: A) The word's indication of its complete real or metaphorical meaning (conformity). B) The word's indication of part of its real or metaphorical meaning (implication). C) The word's indication of another meaning external to its meaning necessary for it rationally or conventionally (requirement).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of verbal conventional indication. The author states the division based on whether speech indicates the whole meaning (mutabaqa), part of it (tadammun), or a necessary concomitant (iltizam). Each is given a technical name. The classification is foundational for understanding how words signify meanings in logic.

Mutabaqa: Definition and Examples
Definition and examples of mutabaqa indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mutabaqa indication in examples and explain why it is called mutabaqa.

Core Idea
Mutabaqa: when a word directly gives its full meaning – literal or metaphorical.

This lesson unpacks the first of the three types of word indication: mutabaqa. The author defines it, explains its etymology, and provides clear examples from both literal and figurative language, including a Quranic verse. Understanding mutabaqa is essential for grasping how words convey complete meanings in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of mutabaqa
Find the sentence that says 'دلالة اللفظ على تمام معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي' – this is the core definition.
Note that it covers both literal and metaphorical meanings.
2
Understand the etymology
The term 'mutabaqa' is derived from the conformity (تطابق) between the word's meaning and the understanding derived from it.
This explains why it is called 'conformity indication'.
3
Identify literal examples
Examples: 'insan' indicates 'hayawan natiq' (the complete essence); 'baqara' in the Quran indicates any cow of that species.
These are cases where the word gives its full literal meaning.
4
Identify metaphorical example
The word 'asad' (lion) in 'I saw a lion fighting' indicates a brave man – this is mutabaqa of the complete metaphorical meaning.
Mutabaqa applies even when the meaning is figurative, as long as the full intended meaning is conveyed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيما يلي شرح هذه الأقسام الثلاثة مع توضيحها بالأمثلة : دلالة المطابقة : عرفنا أن دلالة اللفظ على تمام معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي تسمى دلالة مطابقة وسُمِّيت مطابقة للتطابق الحاصل بين معنى اللفظ وبين الفهم الذي استفيد منه . أمثلة لدلالة المطابقة : كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على الحيوان الناطق، إذ لفظ الإنسان موضوع لكائن فيه الحيوانية والناطقية، وحينما يستفاد من هذا اللفظ تمام هذا المعنى فقد تم التطابق بين معناه وبين الفهم الذي استفيد منه . ومثل لفظ الإنسان جميع أسماء الأجناس حينما تدلُّ في الاستعمال على تمام معانيها الحقيقية أو المجازية، نحو قول الله تعالى : ﴿ إن الله يأمركم أن تذبحوا بقرة ﴾ فلفظة ﴿ بقرة ﴾ اسم جنس سيق ليدلّ على تمام ما وضع له حقيقة، وهو الحيوان المعروف، فأيَّةُ بقرة كانت كافية لتنفيذ الأمر لو ذبحها بنو إسرائيل، ولكنهم شدَّدُوا على أنفسهم في طلب التعيين فشدد الله عليهم، ونحو قولك : رأيت أسداً يقاتل ضمن الصفوف قتال المستميت، فلفظة (أسد) في هذه الجملة سيقت لتدل دلالة مجازية على تمام المعنى المجازي، وهو الإنسان الشجاع . وكدلالة الأعلام على مسمياتها، وكدلالة الأفعال على تمام معانيها الحقيقية أو المجازية، وكدلالة الجمل الكلامية على تمام معانيها أيضاً .
And here is the explanation of these three sections with clarification by examples: Indication of conformity: We have known that the word's indication of its complete real or metaphorical meaning is called conformity indication, and it was called conformity because of the conformity that occurs between the meaning of the word and the understanding derived from it. Examples of conformity indication: Like the word 'human' indicating 'rational animal', for the word 'human' is posited for a being comprising animality and rationality, and when the complete meaning is understood from this word, conformity occurs between its meaning and the understanding derived from it. And like the word 'human' are all genus names when they indicate in usage their complete real or metaphorical meanings, such as the saying of Allah the Most High: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to slaughter a cow.' So the word 'cow' is a genus name brought to indicate the complete what it is literally posited for, which is the known animal. Any cow would have sufficed to execute the command if the Children of Israel slaughtered it, but they made it difficult for themselves by demanding specification, so Allah made it difficult for them. And like your saying: 'I saw a lion fighting in the ranks with desperate fighting,' so the word 'lion' in this sentence is brought to indicate metaphorically the complete metaphorical meaning, which is the brave man. And like the indication of proper names of their referents, and the indication of verbs of their complete real or metaphorical meanings, and the indication of speech sentences of their complete meanings as well.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of mutabaqa indication. The author reiterates the definition and explains the etymology: mutabaqa because of the conformity between the word's meaning and the understanding derived from it. He then gives several examples: first, the word 'insan' indicating its complete essence (hayawan natiq); second, genus names like 'baqara' in the Quranic verse, showing that any cow would suffice; third, the metaphorical use of 'asad' for a brave man; and finally, proper names, verbs, and complete sentences. The examples illustrate that mutabaqa covers both literal and metaphorical indications of the complete meaning.

Key Takeaway

Implicative Connotation: Definition and Types
Defines implicative connotation and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish implicative connotation from literal denotation and identify whether an implication is rational or customary.

Core Idea
Implicative meaning follows necessarily from the literal, either by reason or custom.

Implicative connotation is a powerful tool in Islamic reasoning. It allows us to derive meanings not stated directly but necessarily attached to what is said. This lesson introduces the concept and its two types — rational ('aqli) and customary ('urfi) — as explained in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
Find the direct, explicit meaning (manṭūq) of the expression. For example, 'human' literally means rational animal.
2
Detect the additional connotation
Determine what other meaning is conveyed indirectly. In the example, 'human' implies capability for knowledge and writing.
3
Assess the type of concomitance
Ask: Is the connection between literal and implied meaning necessary by pure reason (rational) or based on repeated customary association (customary)?
Rational: even number implies even divisibility. Customary: long sword-belt implies tall stature.
4
Apply the logician's restriction
Logic only accepts rational implication for certainty. Customary implication is used in rhetoric, uṣūl al-fiqh, and debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة :
كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على قابلية العلم وصنعة الكتابة، فقد علمنا أن لفظ الإنسان موضوع للحيوان الناطق، ولا يدخل في ضمن هذا المعنى قابلية العلم وصنعة الكتابة، ولكن هذه القابلية صفة لازمة للإنسان السوي، فإذا سأل سائل عن شخص : هل هذا قابل للعلم وصنعة الكتابة؟ فقلنا له : (هو إنسان)، فقد أجبناه بالإيجاب، وذلك بمقتضى الدلالة الالتزامية، لأن هذه الصفة صفة لازمة لمعنى الإنسان، واللزوم هذا هو من قبيل اللزوم العرفي لا العقلي .
وكدلالة قولنا : (هذا عدد زوجي) على أنه قابل للقسمة على اثنين دون كسر، لأنه يلزم عقلاً من كونه عدداً زوجياً أنه يتصف بهذه الصفة، وهذا اللزوم هو من قبيل اللزوم العقلي .
ويسأل سائل : هل هذا الشكل الهندسي مربع أو مستطيل؟ .
أ ب
ج د
فنقول له : إذا قسمناه نصفين من منتصف الضلعين (أ ب) و (جـ د) نتج معنا مربعان متطابقان، فقد دللناه بهذا الكلام على أنه مستطيل وليس بمربع، وذلك بمقتضى الدلالة الالتزامية، لأنه يلزم من كونه قابلاً للقسمة على الوجه المبين أن يكون مستطيلاً لا مربعاً، مع أن الكلام ليس في منطوقه ما يدل على أنه مستطيل، وهذا اللزوم هو من قبيل اللزوم العقلي أيضاً .
ونجد هذه الدلالة الالتزامية بنسبة وافرة جداً في الكلام العربي، ونجدها في نصوص القرآن والسنة، وعن طريقها ترتقي البلاغة الكلامية ارتقاءً عظيماً، فمن الكلام العربي قول الشاعر يصف ممدوحه :
طويل النجاد رفيع العماد
كثير الرماد إذا ما شتا
فقد دلّ بطول نجاده ـ وهو حمائل سيفه ـ على طول قامته، ودلّ بارتفاع عماده على عظم بيته وارتفاع مكانته، ودلّ بكثرة رماده على جوده، لأن كثرة الرماد عند العرب تدلُّ على كثرة الطبخ، وهذه تدلُّ على كثرة الآكلين، وكثرة الآكلين عنده تدلُّ على جوده، وكل هذه لوازم عرفية لا عقلية.
ومن الشواهد القرآنية قول الله تعالى في سورة (التغابن ٦٤):
﴿وَإِن تَعْفُوا وَتَصْفَحُوا وَتَغْفِرُوا فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ غَفُورٌ رَّحِيمٌ ١٤﴾.
فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿فإن الله غفور رحيم﴾ الواقع في جواب الشرط يدلُّ عن طريق الدلالة الالتزامية على أن الله يغفر لكم ويرحمكم إن أنتم عفوتم وصفحتم وغفرتم، مع أن هذا المعنى غير مدلول عليه بمنطوق اللفظ، ولكن يلزم من كون الله غفوراً رحيماً أن يكافيء أهل العفو والصفح والمغفرة بالرحمة والغفران. ولذلك حصل الاكتفاء في جواب الشرط بذكر هذين الوصفين دون التصريح بلازمهما، ونظير هذا في القرآن الكريم كثير جداً.
وكل دلالة من هذا القبيل تسمى (دلالة التزامية) وفق التقسيم الذي أوضحناه.
وإذ قد عرفنا أن اللزوم قد يكون لزوماً عقلي الثبوت، أي يحكم العقل المجرد به، وقد يكون لزوماً عرفياً، أي لا يحكم العقل به إلا بعد ملاحظة الواقع، وتكرر مشاهدة اللزوم فيه، دون أن يكون لدى العقل ما يقتضي هذا اللزوم، إذ قد عرفنا هذا فلا بد من التنبيه على أن المعتبر من الدلالة الالتزامية عند المناطقة هي الدلالة الالتزامية العقلية فقط، أما العرفية فلا اعتبار لها عندهم، إنما لها اعتبار في دلالات الكلام بوجه عام، ويعتمد عليها عند علماء البلاغة، ويؤخذ من طريقها مستنبطات فقهية وغيرها عند الأصوليين، وقد يُحتج بها عندهم لدى المناظرة.
Examples:
Like the connotation of the word 'insān' (human) for the capability of knowledge and the craft of writing, for we know that the word 'insān' is assigned to the rational animal, and the capability of knowledge and the craft of writing do not enter within this meaning. But this capability is an inherent attribute of the sound human. So if a questioner asks about a person: 'Is this one capable of knowledge and the craft of writing?' and we say to him: 'He is a human,' we have answered him in the affirmative, and that is by virtue of implicative connotation, because this attribute is an inherent attribute of the meaning of human, and this concomitance is of the type of customary concomitance, not rational.
And like the connotation of our saying: 'This is an even number' for its being divisible into two without a fraction, because it follows rationally from its being an even number that it is characterized by this attribute, and this concomitance is of the type of rational concomitance.
And a questioner asks: 'Is this geometric shape a square or a rectangle?'
A B
C D
So we say to him: 'If we divide it in half from the midpoints of sides (AB) and (CD), we obtain two congruent squares.' We have thereby indicated to him through this statement that it is a rectangle and not a square, and that is by virtue of implicative connotation, because it follows from its being divisible in the mentioned manner that it is a rectangle and not a square, even though the statement does not in its literal wording indicate that it is a rectangle. And this concomitance is also of the type of rational concomitance.
And we find this implicative connotation in very abundant proportion in Arabic speech, and we find it in the texts of the Qur'an and the Sunnah, and through it verbal eloquence rises greatly. So from Arabic speech is the poet's description of his praised one:
'Long of sword-belt, lofty of pillars,
Abundant of ashes when winter comes.'
For he indicated by his long sword-belt—which is the straps of his sword—his tall stature, and indicated by the height of his pillars the greatness of his house and high standing, and indicated by the abundance of his ashes his generosity, because abundance of ashes among the Arabs indicates abundance of cooking, and this indicates abundance of eaters, and abundance of eaters at his place indicates his generosity. And all these are customary concomitants, not rational.
And from the Qur'anic evidences is the saying of Allah Almighty in Surah At-Taghabun (64:14):
'And if you pardon and overlook and forgive, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.'
For His saying Almighty: 'then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful' occurring in the answer to the condition indicates through implicative connotation that Allah forgives you and has mercy on you if you forgive and overlook and pardon, even though this meaning is not indicated by the literal wording, but it follows from the fact that Allah is Forgiving and Merciful that He rewards the people of pardon, overlooking, and forgiveness with mercy and forgiveness. Therefore, it sufficed in the answer to the condition to mention these two attributes without explicitly stating their necessary consequence, and parallels to this in the Noble Qur'an are very many.
And every connotation of this kind is called 'implicative connotation' according to the division we have explained.
And since we have known that concomitance may be rational in its affirmation, i.e., pure reason judges it, and may be customary, i.e., reason does not judge it except after observing reality and the repeated observation of concomitance in it, without reason having anything that necessitates this concomitance, now that we have known this, we must draw attention to the fact that the considered [type] of implicative connotation among the logicians is only rational implicative connotation. As for customary, it has no consideration among them. Rather, it has consideration in the connotations of speech in general, and it is relied upon by scholars of rhetoric, and juristic deductions and others are taken from its path by the legal theorists, and it may be used as evidence among them in debate.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates 'al-dalala al-iltizamiyya' (implicative connotation). The author begins by giving examples to distinguish between rational and customary concomitance. The first example: the word 'insān' (human) literally means 'rational animal', but it customarily implies the capability for knowledge and writing. This is customary because the connection is based on usual experience, not logical necessity. The second example: 'even number' rationally implies divisibility by two without remainder; this is a rational necessary connection. The third example uses a geometric shape: if dividing a shape from midpoints of opposite sides yields two congruent squares, it implies the shape is a rectangle, not a square. This is also rational. The author then notes that Arabic speech, including poetry and the Qur'an, is rich in implicative connotation. The poetic example: describing a person's long sword-belt implies tall stature (customary), and abundant ashes implies generosity (customary chain). The Qur'anic example: Allah's attributes 'Forgiving and Merciful' in the answer to a condition imply that He will forgive those who forgive, a rational implication (since mercy necessitates rewarding mercy). The author concludes by distinguishing the two types of concomitance: rational (judged by pure reason) and customary (based on repeated observation). Importantly, logicians only accept rational implicative connotation as valid in logic, while customary is used by rhetoricians, legal theorists, and in debate.

Key Terms
دلالة التزامية
لزوم عقلي
لزوم عرفي

Checkpoint

A) Rational
B) Customary
C) Both rational and customary
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Implicative connotation is meaning necessarily attached to literal meaning, divided into rational (logically necessary) and customary (based on repeated experience).

?

Implicative Connotation in Arabic and Qur'anic Examples
Illustrates implicative connotation with Arabic poetry and Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze classical Arabic poetry and Quranic verses to identify and explain implicative connotations.

Core Idea
Implicative connotation is a rich source of eloquence in Arabic, especially in poetry and the Qur'an.

The passage provides vivid examples of implicative connotation in Arabic speech and the Qur'an. By examining these examples, we learn how implied meanings elevate discourse and convey messages indirectly yet powerfully. This lesson walks through the poetic and Quranic examples step by step.

Guided Path
1
Examine the poetic example
Read the line 'طويل النجاد رفيع العماد كثير الرماد إذا ما شتا'. Identify each implied meaning: long sword-belt -> tall stature; lofty pillars -> high status; abundant ashes -> generosity.
These are customary implications based on Arab customs.
2
Trace the chain of implications
The poet uses multiple implicative steps: abundant ashes -> abundant cooking -> many guests -> generosity. Each step is customary.
The listener's knowledge of Arab customs makes the chain comprehensible.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
Read the verse (64:14): 'And if you pardon and overlook and forgive, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' The answer to the condition only mentions Allah's attributes, but the implication is that He will forgive you.
This is rational: a Forgiving and Merciful God necessarily rewards similar behavior.
4
Compare the types
The poetic implications are customary (based on Arab custom), while the Quranic implication is rational (based on logical necessity from God's attributes).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة :
كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على قابلية العلم وصنعة الكتابة، فقد علمنا أن لفظ الإنسان موضوع للحيوان الناطق، ولا يدخل في ضمن هذا المعنى قابلية العلم وصنعة الكتابة، ولكن هذه القابلية صفة لازمة للإنسان السوي، فإذا سأل سائل عن شخص : هل هذا قابل للعلم وصنعة الكتابة؟ فقلنا له : (هو إنسان)، فقد أجبناه بالإيجاب، وذلك بمقتضى الدلالة الالتزامية، لأن هذه الصفة صفة لازمة لمعنى الإنسان، واللزوم هذا هو من قبيل اللزوم العرفي لا العقلي .
وكدلالة قولنا : (هذا عدد زوجي) على أنه قابل للقسمة على اثنين دون كسر، لأنه يلزم عقلاً من كونه عدداً زوجياً أنه يتصف بهذه الصفة، وهذا اللزوم هو من قبيل اللزوم العقلي .
ويسأل سائل : هل هذا الشكل الهندسي مربع أو مستطيل؟ .
أ ب
ج د
فنقول له : إذا قسمناه نصفين من منتصف الضلعين (أ ب) و (جـ د) نتج معنا مربعان متطابقان، فقد دللناه بهذا الكلام على أنه مستطيل وليس بمربع، وذلك بمقتضى الدلالة الالتزامية، لأنه يلزم من كونه قابلاً للقسمة على الوجه المبين أن يكون مستطيلاً لا مربعاً، مع أن الكلام ليس في منطوقه ما يدل على أنه مستطيل، وهذا اللزوم هو من قبيل اللزوم العقلي أيضاً .
ونجد هذه الدلالة الالتزامية بنسبة وافرة جداً في الكلام العربي، ونجدها في نصوص القرآن والسنة، وعن طريقها ترتقي البلاغة الكلامية ارتقاءً عظيماً، فمن الكلام العربي قول الشاعر يصف ممدوحه :
طويل النجاد رفيع العماد
كثير الرماد إذا ما شتا
فقد دلّ بطول نجاده ـ وهو حمائل سيفه ـ على طول قامته، ودلّ بارتفاع عماده على عظم بيته وارتفاع مكانته، ودلّ بكثرة رماده على جوده، لأن كثرة الرماد عند العرب تدلُّ على كثرة الطبخ، وهذه تدلُّ على كثرة الآكلين، وكثرة الآكلين عنده تدلُّ على جوده، وكل هذه لوازم عرفية لا عقلية.
ومن الشواهد القرآنية قول الله تعالى في سورة (التغابن ٦٤):
﴿وَإِن تَعْفُوا وَتَصْفَحُوا وَتَغْفِرُوا فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ غَفُورٌ رَّحِيمٌ ١٤﴾.
فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿فإن الله غفور رحيم﴾ الواقع في جواب الشرط يدلُّ عن طريق الدلالة الالتزامية على أن الله يغفر لكم ويرحمكم إن أنتم عفوتم وصفحتم وغفرتم، مع أن هذا المعنى غير مدلول عليه بمنطوق اللفظ، ولكن يلزم من كون الله غفوراً رحيماً أن يكافيء أهل العفو والصفح والمغفرة بالرحمة والغفران. ولذلك حصل الاكتفاء في جواب الشرط بذكر هذين الوصفين دون التصريح بلازمهما، ونظير هذا في القرآن الكريم كثير جداً.
وكل دلالة من هذا القبيل تسمى (دلالة التزامية) وفق التقسيم الذي أوضحناه.
وإذ قد عرفنا أن اللزوم قد يكون لزوماً عقلي الثبوت، أي يحكم العقل المجرد به، وقد يكون لزوماً عرفياً، أي لا يحكم العقل به إلا بعد ملاحظة الواقع، وتكرر مشاهدة اللزوم فيه، دون أن يكون لدى العقل ما يقتضي هذا اللزوم، إذ قد عرفنا هذا فلا بد من التنبيه على أن المعتبر من الدلالة الالتزامية عند المناطقة هي الدلالة الالتزامية العقلية فقط، أما العرفية فلا اعتبار لها عندهم، إنما لها اعتبار في دلالات الكلام بوجه عام، ويعتمد عليها عند علماء البلاغة، ويؤخذ من طريقها مستنبطات فقهية وغيرها عند الأصوليين، وقد يُحتج بها عندهم لدى المناظرة.
Examples:
Like the connotation of the word 'insān' (human) for the capability of knowledge and the craft of writing, for we know that the word 'insān' is assigned to the rational animal, and the capability of knowledge and the craft of writing do not enter within this meaning. But this capability is an inherent attribute of the sound human. So if a questioner asks about a person: 'Is this one capable of knowledge and the craft of writing?' and we say to him: 'He is a human,' we have answered him in the affirmative, and that is by virtue of implicative connotation, because this attribute is an inherent attribute of the meaning of human, and this concomitance is of the type of customary concomitance, not rational.

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates 'al-dalala al-iltizamiyya' (implicative connotation). The author begins by giving examples to distinguish between rational and customary concomitance. The first example: the word 'insān' (human) literally means 'rational animal', but it customarily implies the capability for knowledge and writing. This is customary because the connection is based on usual experience, not logical necessity. The second example: 'even number' rationally implies divisibility by two without remainder; this is a rational necessary connection. The third example uses a geometric shape: if dividing a shape from midpoints of opposite sides yields two congruent squares, it implies the shape is a rectangle, not a square. This is also rational. The author concludes by distinguishing the two types of concomitance: rational (judged by pure reason) and customary (based on repeated observation). Implicative Connotation in Islamic Sciences: Logic vs Uṣūl al-Fiqh
Draws disciplinary boundaries for implicative connotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians restrict implicative connotation to rational implication and contrast this with its broader use in uṣūl al-fiqh and rhetoric.

Core Idea
Logicians demand certainty; only rational implication provides it, while other fields accept customary implication as valid.

The passage concludes by noting a key distinction: logicians only accept rational implicative connotation because it yields certainty, while scholars of rhetoric, uṣūl al-fiqh, and debate also use customary implication. This lesson explores the reasons and implications of this disciplinary boundary.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types
Rational implication is necessary by pure reason; customary implication is based on repeated observation without logical necessity.
The passage gives examples: even number (rational), human capability (customary).
2
Understand the logician's standard
Logicians seek yaqīn (certainty) in proofs. Customary implication can only provide ẓann (probable knowledge), so it is excluded from formal logical reasoning.
The passage states: 'المعتبر من الدلالة الالتزامية عند المناطقة هي الدلالة الالتزامية العقلية فقط'.
3
See how other sciences use customary implication
Rhetoricians use customary implication for eloquence. Uṣūlīs (legal theorists) use it for juristic deductions and evidence in debate.
The passage mentions 'مستنبطات فقهية' and 'المناظرة'.
4
Apply the distinction
When you encounter an implicative connotation in an Islamic text, determine whether the connection is logically necessary or culturally based. That will tell you which discipline can legitimately use it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة :

Examples:

But this capability is an inherent attribute of the sound human. C D
So we say to him: 'If we divide it in half from the midpoints of sides (AB) and (CD), we obtain two congruent squares.' We have thereby indicated to him through this statement that it is a rectangle and not a square, and that is by virtue of implicative connotation, because it follows from its being divisible in the mentioned manner that it is a rectangle and not a square, even though the statement does not in its literal wording indicate that it is a rectangle. And this concomitance is also of the type of rational concomitance.
And we find this implicative connotation in very abundant proportion in Arabic speech, and we find it in the texts of the Qur'an and the Sunnah, and through it verbal eloquence rises greatly. So from Arabic speech is the poet's description of his praised one:
'Long of sword-belt, lofty of pillars,
Abundant of ashes when winter comes.'
For he indicated by his long sword-belt—which is the straps of his sword—his tall stature, and indicated by the height of his pillars the greatness of his house and high standing, and indicated by the abundance of his ashes his generosity, because abundance of ashes among the Arabs indicates abundance of cooking, and this indicates abundance of eaters, and abundance of eaters at his place indicates his generosity. And all these are customary concomitants, not rational.
And from the Qur'anic evidences is the saying of Allah Almighty in Surah At-Taghabun (64:14):
'And if you pardon and overlook and forgive, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.'
For His saying Almighty: 'then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful' occurring in the answer to the condition indicates through implicative connotation that Allah forgives you and has mercy on you if you forgive and overlook and pardon, even though this meaning is not indicated by the literal wording, but it follows from the fact that Allah is Forgiving and Merciful that He rewards the people of pardon, overlooking, and forgiveness with mercy and forgiveness. Therefore, it sufficed in the answer to the condition to mention these two attributes without explicitly stating their necessary consequence, and parallels to this in the Noble Qur'an are very many.
And every connotation of this kind is called 'implicative connotation' according to the division we have explained.
And since we have known that concomitance may be rational in its affirmation, i.e., pure reason judges it, and may be customary, i.e., reason does not judge it except after observing reality and the repeated observation of concomitance in it, without reason having anything that necessitates this concomitance, now that we have known this, we must draw attention to the fact that the considered [type] of implicative connotation among the logicians is only rational implicative connotation. As for customary, it has no consideration among them. Rather, it has consideration in the connotations of speech in general, and it is relied upon by scholars of rhetoric, and juristic deductions and others are taken from its path by the legal theorists, and it may be used as evidence among them in debate.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates 'al-dalala al-iltizamiyya' (implicative connotation). The author begins by giving examples to distinguish between rational and customary concomitance. The first example: the word 'insān' (human) literally means 'rational animal', but it customarily implies the capability for knowledge and writing. This is customary because the connection is based on usual experience, not logical necessity. The second example: 'even number' rationally implies divisibility by two without remainder; this is a rational necessary connection. The third example uses a geometric shape: if dividing a shape from midpoints of opposite sides yields two congruent squares, it implies the shape is a rectangle, not a square. This is also rational. The author then notes that Arabic speech, including poetry and the Qur'an, is rich in implicative connotation. The poetic example: describing a person's long sword-belt implies tall stature (customary), and abundant ashes implies generosity (customary chain). The Qur'anic example: Allah's attributes 'Forgiving and Merciful' in the answer to a condition imply that He will forgive those who forgive, a rational implication (since mercy necessitates rewarding mercy). The author concludes by distinguishing the two types of concomitance: rational (judged by pure reason) and customary (based on repeated observation). Importantly, logicians only accept rational implicative connotation as valid in logic, while customary is used by rhetoricians, legal theorists, and in debate.

دلالة التزامية عرفية

Key Takeaway
The validity of implicative connotation depends on the discipline: logicians demand rational certainty, while other Islamic sciences accept customary accompaniment for probabilistic inference.

The Four Key Definitions: Whole, Universal, Part, Particular
Author provides concise definitions of kull, kulli, juz', and juz'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the definitions of kull, kulli, juz', and juz'i using Islamic examples from terminology in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Four definitions separate every concept into whole/part and universal/particular.

This passage is a crisp summary of four definitions that are the bedrock of logical analysis. Memorizing these distinctions will help you recognize whether a term applies to many or one, and whether it is a component or a composite. In this lesson, we will break down each definition and apply it to Islamic legal concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify each term
Find the four terms in the Arabic: الكل, الكلي, الجزء, الجزئي. Note their English equivalents: whole, universal, part, particular.
These terms are often confused with each other, so focus on the exact wording.
2
Understand the definition of al-kull (whole)
The whole is defined as 'that which is composed of two or more parts.' This is a compositional definition. For example, a house is a whole composed of walls, roof, and door.
Remember: whole is the composite, not the component.
3
Understand the definition of al-kulli (universal)
The universal is defined as 'a concept that does not prevent sharing in the mind.' That means multiple individuals can be described by it. For example, 'human' is universal because it applies to Zayd, 'Amr, and many others.
The universal is about applicability, not composition.
4
Understand the definitions of al-juz' and al-juz'i (part and particular)
The part is 'that from which, together with another, a whole is composed.' For example, a wall is a part of a house. The particular is 'a concept whose meaning does not admit of sharing in the mind' – it applies to only one individual, such as 'Khalid' or 'this house'.
Note the subtle difference: part is a component of a whole; particular is a concept applying to one.
5
Test with Islamic examples
Consider 'worship' ('ibada): it is universal because many acts (prayer, fasting, etc.) fall under it. 'The prayer of Fajr' is particular because it refers to a specific prayer. 'Faith' (iman) as a whole is composed of belief, speech, and action – so it is a whole. 'Belief' (tasdiq) is a part of faith.
Use these examples to solidify the distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاصة تعريفات:
١ - الكل: ما تركب من جزأين فأكثر.
٢ - الكلي: ما لا يمنع نفس تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه.
٣ - الجزء: ما تركب منه ومن غيره كل.
٤ - الجزئي: ما كان معناه لا يقبل في الذهن الاشتراك.
Summary of definitions: 1 - The whole: that which is composed of two or more parts. 2 - The universal: that whose very concept does not prevent sharing in it. 3 - The part: that from which, together with another, a whole is composed. 4 - The particular: that whose meaning does not admit of sharing in the mind.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a compressed summary of four key definitions that the author has previously explained. The first definition: 'al-kull' (the whole) is something composed of two or more parts. For example, a house is composed of walls, roof, and door. The second definition: 'al-kulli' (the universal) is a concept that does not prevent multiple individuals from sharing it; e.g., 'human' applies to Zayd, 'Amr, etc. The third definition: 'al-juz'' (the part) is that from which, together with something else, a whole is made. For instance, a wall is a part of a house because when combined with roof, door, etc., it forms the whole house. The fourth definition: 'al-juz'i' (the particular) is a concept that does not admit of sharing in the mind; it applies to only one individual, such as 'Khalid' or 'this house'. These definitions are meant to be memorized as foundational tools for logical analysis.

The Tree of Genera and Species: Relational Classification
Author presents a tree diagram classifying genera and species from highest to lowest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the highest genus, lowest species, and intermediate terms in a hierarchical classification and explain how a single term can function as both a species and a genus depending on its position.

Core Idea
Every term except the extremes is simultaneously a species and a genus — direction matters.

This passage uses a tree diagram to visualise the classification of logical terms from the most universal ('mental perceptibles') to the most specific ('human'). Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for constructing proper definitions, as every real definition requires a proximate genus and a specific differentia. The tree reveals that most terms are 'relative species' (naw' idafi): they serve as species for higher terms and as genera for lower terms. By parsing this tree, you will grasp how genus and species are not absolute but relational categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the highest genus (jins al-ajnas)
Find the term at the top of the tree: 'mental perceptibles' (al-mudrakat al-dhihniyya). This is the genus of all other terms and has no genus above it.
In this tree, the highest genus is 'mental perceptibles'; in other systems it may be the ten categories or 'substance'.
2
Identify the lowest species (naw' al-anwa')
Locate the most specific terms at the bottom: 'human', 'horse', 'gazelle', etc. The passage states that human (and its equivalents) is the species of species — it has no species below it, only varieties (asnaf).
Varieties (e.g., 'tall human', 'short human') are not considered logical species because they lack a differentia.
3
Distinguish proximate and remote genera
For any term, identify its immediate superior (proximate genus, jins qarib) and higher superiors (remote genus, jins ba'id). Example: 'animal' is the proximate genus of 'human'; 'body' is a remote genus of 'human' because there is an intermediate ('animal').
The passage lists several chains: e.g., 'mental perceptible' → 'possible' → 'existent' → 'substance' → 'body' → 'growing' → 'animal' → 'human'.
4
Identify intermediate terms and their three functions
Terms like 'possible', 'existent', 'substance', 'body', 'growing' are intermediaries. Each is: (1) an additional species (naw' idafi) relative to what is above it, (2) a proximate genus to what is directly below, and (3) a remote genus to terms further down.
The passage explicitly says these terms have three attributes: additional species, proximate genus, remote genus.
5
Apply the relational nature to a fiqh example
Consider 'worship' ('ibada) as a genus. Its species include 'salah', 'sawm', etc. 'Salah' is a species of 'worship' and a genus for its varieties (fard, sunnah, etc.). 'Worship' is a species of 'religious acts', which is a species of 'acts'. This mirrors the tree structure.
This exercise shows that the same logical relations operate in Islamic legal theory.

And the following approximate tree reveals these facts: Highest genus, or the genus of genera: mental perceptibles. Under it: additional species, i.e., in relation to what is above it: possible, necessary, impossible. A proximate genus in relation to what is directly under it. A remote genus in relation to what is below that. Like what is above it: existent, non-existent. Like what is above it: substance, accident. Like what is above it: body, non-body. Like what is above it: growing, non-growing. Like what is above it, except that: genus not animal, non-animal. Under it a genus, and so on to the rest of animals. The species of species: human, horse, gazelle, giraffe, raven. From this tree we observe that possible, existent, substance, body, and growing are intermediaries, each having three attributes: additional species, proximate genus, remote genus. And that the mental perceptible is the genus of genera, and that animal is the lowest of genera, and that human and what is in its rank are the lowest of species, and it is the species of species, and it is the true species, and the divisions of human are varieties and not species.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a tree diagram that classifies logical terms from the highest genus (jins al-ajnas) down to the lowest species (naw' al-anwa'). The author uses this visual to explain the relative nature of genus and species: a term can be an additional species (naw' idafi) relative to what is above it, a proximate genus (jins qarib) to what is directly below, and a remote genus (jins ba'id) to terms further down. Examples include 'possible, necessary, impossible' under 'mental perceptibles', and then a chain down to 'animal' as the lowest genus, with 'human' as the species of species. The tree uses categories from metaphysical and natural classifications, such as existent/non-existent, substance/accident, body/non-body, growing/non-growing, and animal/non-animal. The key observation is that intermediate terms have three functions: they are species of higher genera, genera of lower species, and can be proximate or remote. The passage clarifies that animal is the genus immediately above human, and human is the true species (naw' haqiqi) whose subdivisions are only varieties (asnaf), not species.

Key Terms
جنس الأجناس (jins al-ajnas): The highest genus, which has no genus above it; here identified with 'mental perceptibles' (al-mudrakat al-dhihniyya). [Note: In later logic, jins al-ajnas often refers to the ten categories (al-maqulat al-'ashr), not mental perceptibles.]
نوع الأنواع (naw' al-anwa'): The lowest species, which has no species below it; here identified with 'human' and its equivalents.
نوع إضافي (naw' idafi): A relative species: a term that is a species of a higher genus and itself a genus for lower terms.
جنس قريب (jins qarib): A genus that is directly above its species, with no intermediate genus.
جنس بعيد (jins ba'id): A genus that is above its species through one or more intermediate genera.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The tree diagram shows that genus and species are relational: a term can be a species of a higher genus and a genus of a lower species, with only the highest genus and lowest species being absolute.

The Equivocal Universal (al-Mushakkik): Definition and Examples
Author explains the concept of al-mushakkik with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushakkik universal and distinguish it from univocal and equivocal terms.

Core Idea
Mushakkik universals have a single meaning that varies in intensity across individuals.

In this lesson, we explore a special type of universal called al-mushakkik (the equivocal/graduated universal). Unlike univocal terms where the meaning is uniform across all individuals, mushakkik terms have a meaning that exists in varying degrees. The classical examples of light and whiteness illustrate this concept. Understanding mushakkik is crucial for precise definition and argumentation in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic of mushakkik
The meaning of the term exists in its individuals in a divergent, non-uniform manner, with varying intensity.
This is the core feature that distinguishes mushakkik from other universals.
2
Examine the example of 'light' (nur)
Light is a mushakkik because sunlight, moonlight, and lamplight all share the same essence of light but differ in strength.
The example shows variation within a single meaning.
3
Examine the example of 'whiteness' (bayad)
Similarly, whiteness in snow is more intense than in ivory, showing variation in degree.
This reinforces the concept with another familiar example.
4
Understand the reason for the name 'mushakkik'
It causes uncertainty (tashkik) in the observer: is it univocal (uniform) or equivocal (different meanings)? It lands between them.
The name reflects the hesitation it produces.
5
Distinguish from univocal and equivocal
Univocal: single meaning, uniform (e.g., human). Equivocal: same word, different meanings (e.g., 'ayn). Mushakkik: single meaning, variable intensity.
This comparison solidifies the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمثلة :
١ - مثل كلمة : (نور) فإن وجود معنى هذا اللفظ في أفراده وجود متفاوت غير متوافق، إذ نور الشمس أقوى من نور القمر، ونورهما أقوى من نور المصباح الكهربائي، وكلها ذات نور أقوى من نور شمعة أو عود ثقاب .
ب - ومثل كلمة : (بياض) أو (أبيض) فهذا كلي معناه في الثلج أشد منه في عظم العاج، ومن المشاهد في الأشياء البيضاء أن نسبة بياضها متفاوتة لا متوافقة .
وما فيه نسبة التشكيك هذه يسمّى (المشكّك) وسبب هذه التسمية أن نسبة وجود المعنى في الأفراد تشكّكُ الناظر فيها بين أمرين : هل هي متوافقة فيلحقها بالمتواطىء؟ أو هي مختلفة اختلافاً كلياً فيلحقها بالمشترك؟ (وهو الذي اتحد لفظه وتعدد معناه)، فلما كان الناظر فيه يخيل إليه أنه يتردد بين المتواطىء والمشترك كان جديراً بأن يسمى مشكّكاً، إذ النسبة توقع بالتشكك .
Examples: 1- Like the word 'light': the existence of the meaning of this word in its individuals is a divergent existence, not uniform, for the light of the sun is stronger than the light of the moon, and both of them are stronger than the light of an electric lamp, and all of them have light stronger than the light of a candle or a match. B- And like the word 'whiteness' or 'white': this is a universal whose meaning in snow is more intense than it is in ivory, and it is observed in white things that the proportion of their whiteness is divergent, not uniform. And that which has this relation of tashkik is called 'al-mushakkik', and the reason for this naming is that the relation of the existence of the meaning in individuals makes the observer uncertain between two matters: Is it uniform, so he attaches it to the univocal? Or is it completely different, so he attaches it to the equivocal? (which is the one whose word is one and its meaning is multiple). Since the observer imagines that he is hesitating between the univocal and the equivocal, it is worthy of being called mushakkik, because the relation causes uncertainty.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines a type of universal known as al-mushakkik (the equivocal or graduated universal). The author begins by giving two examples: the word 'light' and the word 'whiteness'. In these cases, the meaning exists in different individuals with varying degrees or intensities. For instance, sunlight is stronger than moonlight, and both are stronger than lamplight. Similarly, the whiteness of snow is more intense than that of ivory. This variation makes the universal 'mushakkik'. The reason for this name is that the observer is uncertain: is this meaning uniform across individuals (like a univocal term) or is it completely different (like an equivocal term)? Because of this hesitation, the relation is called tashkik (uncertainty). This concept is important in distinguishing between types of universals in logic.

Key Terms
المشكك

Key Takeaway
Al-mushakkik is a universal whose single meaning is realized in different individuals with varying degrees or intensity, positioned between univocal and equivocal.

Definition of Synonymy (al-Taraduf)
Author defines al-taraduf (synonymy) as multiple words sharing a single meaning, with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify synonymy (taraduf) in Arabic and explain its logical structure based on the definition: meaning is one, words are multiple.

Core Idea
Synonymy is the relation where multiple words share one meaning – meaning is one, words are multiple.

In this passage, the author introduces the seventh logical relation among words: synonymy. This is a crucial concept for understanding how language can indicate meaning. We'll see exactly what makes words synonymous and how to distinguish genuine synonymy from other relations. The examples provided, such as 'insan' and 'bashar', illustrate the core idea clearly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum (al-taraduf)
The passage explicitly defines 'al-taraduf' as a relation between words. Note that it is the seventh logical relation discussed.
2
Extract the condition for synonymy
Synonymy occurs when each word indicates a single meaning, and they share that meaning. The key phrase: 'meaning is one, words are multiple'.
3
Analyze the examples
Check the three examples: (a) 'insan' and 'bashar' both mean human; (b) 'asad', 'layth', and 'qaswarah' all mean lion; (c) 'zakhar', 'tama', and 'tamalla' all mean to overflow. Verify that each set has a single shared meaning.
4
Understand the terminology
The words that share a meaning are called 'mutaradifah' (synonyms). The term 'taraduf' literally means 'following one another in succession', reflecting how synonyms mutually indicate the same meaning.
This etymology helps remember the concept.
5
Test the definition's completeness
Does this definition cover all synonyms? Yes, because it only requires shared meaning. It excludes homonyms (one word, multiple meanings) and antonyms (opposite meanings).
Also note that synonyms may have nuanced differences, but the core meaning is shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة السابعة : (الترادف) وهي نسبة لفظ إلى لفظ من جهة دلالة كلّ منهما على معنى واحد يشتركان في الدلالة عليه، ففي الترادف يتحد المعنى ويتعدد اللفظ، أي : يكون للمعنى الواحد عدة ألفاظ كل منها يدلّ عليه .
الأمثلة :
أ - مثل كلمتي : (إنسان) و (بشر) فمعنى كل من اللفظين واحد لهما .
ب - ومثل كلمات : (أسد) و (ليث) و (قسورة) فمعنى كل منها هو معنى اللفظ الآخر.
جـ - ومثل كلمات : (زَخَر) و (طَمَا) و (تَمَلأ) فمعنى كل من هذه الأفعال هو معنى الفعل الآخر.
والألفاظ المشتركة في أنها تدل على معنى واحد تسمى (مترادفة) إذ النسبة بينها هي الترادف، وسبب هذه التسمية أن الألفاظ فيها تترادف، أي : تتوالى ويتابع بعضها بعضاً في الدلالة على معنى واحد .
The seventh relation: Synonymy (al-taraduf). It is a relation of one word to another in terms of each of them indicating a single meaning which they share in indicating. In synonymy, the meaning is one and the words are multiple, i.e., for one meaning there are several words, each of which indicates it. Examples: A- Like the two words 'insan' and 'bashar': the meaning of each of the two words is one for both. B- And like the words 'asad', 'layth', and 'qaswarah': the meaning of each of them is the meaning of the other word. C- And like the words 'zakhar', 'tama', and 'tamalla': the meaning of each of these verbs is the meaning of the other verb. And the words that share in indicating one meaning are called 'mutaradifah' (synonyms), because the relation between them is taraduf, and the reason for this naming is that the words in it follow one another, i.e., they come in succession and some follow others in indicating one meaning.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh logical relation: al-taraduf (synonymy). Synonymy occurs when two or more words refer to the same meaning. The author emphasizes that in synonymy, the meaning is unified while the words are multiple. Three examples are given: 1) 'insan' and 'bashar' both mean human. 2) 'asad', 'layth', and 'qaswarah' all mean lion. 3) 'zakhar', 'tama', and 'tamalla' all mean to overflow or be full. The words that share a single meaning are called mutaradifah (synonyms), and the term taraduf comes from the idea of words following one another in succession in indicating that meaning.

Key Terms
الترادف
مترادفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) 'Tawil (long) and qasir (short) - opposite meanings.
D) 'Nur (light) and nur (light) - same word, same meaning.

Key Takeaway
Synonymy (al-taraduf) is defined as multiple words sharing a single meaning, with the meaning being one and the words multiple.

Understanding Homonymy (al-Ishtirak) in Arabic Lexical Semantics
Author defines homonymy as one word with multiple meanings, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushtarak (homonym) and distinguish it from a mutaradif (synonym) by analyzing the word-meaning relationship.

Core Idea
Homonymy: one word, many meanings—opposite of synonymy.

In the eighth relation of his logical work, the author defines homonymy (al-ishtirak). This concept is crucial for understanding how a single word can indicate multiple distinct meanings. Unlike synonymy where multiple words share one meaning, homonymy involves one word pointing to several meanings. The author provides examples from Arabic to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of homonymy
The author states: 'في الاشتراك يتحد اللفظ ويتعدَّد المعنى' (the word is one and the meanings are multiple). This is the opposite of synonymy.
2
Examine the examples given
List the examples: 'ayn (عين) for eye/sun/gold/essence; 'jawn' (جون) for black/red/white; 'qur' (قرء) for menstruation/purity. Each demonstrates one word with multiple distinct senses.
3
Understand the cause of homonymy
The author mentions similarity between referents as a possible cause, using 'rijl' (رجل) for living being/table/chair. Also speculation of a general meaning or transfer.
4
Apply the concept to a new example
Take a word like 'umma' (أمة) which can mean nation, religious community, or a period of time. Check if it fits the definition of mushtarak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة الثامنة: (الاشتراك) وهي نسبة معنىً إلى معنىً من جهة اشتراكهما في لفظ واحد يدل عليهما، ففي الاشتراك يتحد اللفظ ويتعدَّد المعنى، على عكس الترادف.
الأمثلة :
أ - مثل كلمة : (عين) فهي موضوعة لعدة معان في اللغة؛ فمن معانيها الحاسة التي يكون بها الإبصار، ومن معانيها الشمس، ومن معانيها الذهب، ومن معانيها ذات الشيء، وهكذا إلى معان أخرى موجودة في معاجم اللغة.
ب - ومثل كلمة : (جَوْن) فهي تطلق في اللغة على الأسود وعلى الأحمر الخالص وعلى الأبيض.
جـ - ومثل كلمة : (قُرْء) فهي تطلق في اللغة على الحيض، وتطلق أيضاً على الطهر.
واللفظ الذي يدل بالاشتراك على معان متعددة يسمى (مشتركاً) لأن النسبة التي يدل عليها هذا اللفظ هي الاشتراك، أي هي مشتركة في لفظ واحد يدلُّ على كل منها.
وقد يكون سبب الاشتراك التشابه بين المسميين المختلفين كالرَّجل للحي الذي يمشي، وللمنضدة، وللكرسي، وربما كان مثل هذا من قبيل الموضوع لمعنى عام شامل لهذه المختلفات، أو المنقول لهذا المعنى العام.
The eighth relation: Homonymy (al-ishtirak). It is a relation of one meaning to another meaning in terms of their sharing in one word that indicates both. In homonymy, the word is one and the meanings are multiple, opposite to synonymy. Examples: A- Like the word 'ayn': it is assigned to several meanings in language; among its meanings are the sense by which sight occurs, the sun, gold, the essence of a thing, and so on to other meanings found in language dictionaries. B- And like the word 'jawn': it is used in language for black, pure red, and white. C- And like the word 'qur': it is used in language for menstruation, and it is also used for purity. And the word that indicates multiple meanings by homonymy is called 'mushtarak' (homonym), because the relation indicated by this word is ishtirak, i.e., the meanings share in one word that indicates each of them. And the cause of homonymy may be the similarity between the different things named, like 'rijl' for the living being that walks, and for a table, and for a chair; perhaps such is of the type of being assigned to a general meaning inclusive of these different things, or transferred to this general meaning.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth relation: al-ishtirak (homonymy), which is the opposite of synonymy. In homonymy, a single word has multiple distinct meanings. The author provides three examples: 'ayn' (eye, sun, gold, essence), 'jawn' (black, red, white), and 'qur' (menstruation, purity). Words of this type are called mushtarak (homonymous). The author also discusses a possible cause: similarity between the referents, such as the word 'rijl' being used for a living being that walks, a table, and a chair, perhaps because they all share a general notion of 'support' or 'something that stands'. Alternatively, the word may have been transferred from a general meaning to specific ones. This illustrates how homonymy arises in language.

Key Terms
الاشتراك
مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The word 'sa'ala' meaning both to ask and to beg.
C) The word 'qur' meaning both menstruation and purity.
D) The word 'lahn' meaning both melody and grammatical error.

Key Takeaway
Homonymy is a one-to-many word-meaning relation opposite to synonymy, essential for accurate lexical analysis in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Understanding Contrariety (al-Takhaluf)
Author defines al-takhaluf with conditions and example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-takhaluf and test whether a given pair of meanings is contrary using the conditions of coexistence and mutual absence.

Core Idea
Contrariety means two meanings can both be present or both be absent—no forced exclusion.

In Islamic logic, relations between concepts are classified into nine types. One key type is contrariety (al-takhaluf), which allows two meanings to coexist or both be absent. This is different from contradiction (tadaad), where one must be present. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper definition and reasoning in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Identify the phrase defining takhaluf: 'نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان'. Note the two key conditions: possibility of coexistence (ijtima') and possibility of removal (irtifa').
Both conditions are about possibility, not necessity.
2
Understand the conditions
Coexistence means both can be true of the same subject at the same time. Removal means both can be false of the same subject at the same time. These are not about necessity, but possibility.
Unity of time and place ensures we are comparing the same situation.
3
Contrast with other relations
For example, in contradiction, one must be present and the other absent; they cannot both be present or both be absent. In contrast, contrariety allows both states.
This distinguishes takhaluf from tadaad (opposition) where coexistence is impossible.
4
Apply to the given example
Length and whiteness can coexist (a long white object) and both be absent (a short black object). So they are contrary.
The passage gives this as an explicit example.
5
Test with a new example
Consider 'ilm (knowledge) and 'amal (action). They can coexist (a knowledgeable person who acts) and both be absent (an ignorant person who doesn't act). So they are contrary.
This shows the practical application in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة التاسعة : (التخالف) وهي نسبة بين معنى ومعنى آخر من جهة إمكان اجتماعهما وإمكان ارتفاعهما مع اتحاد المكان والزمان، أي: يمكن اجتماعهما معاً في شيء واحد في زمان واحد، ويمكن ارتفاعهما معاً عن شيء واحد في زمان واحد، ويقصد بارتفاعهما انتفاؤهما وعدم وجودهما.
الأمثلة :
أ - مثل : (الطول والبياض) فهما متخالفان، والنسبة بينهما هي
The ninth relation: Contrariety (al-takhaluf). It is a relation between one meaning and another meaning in terms of the possibility of their coexistence and the possibility of their removal together, with unity of place and time. That is, they can coexist together in one thing at one time, and they can both be removed from one thing at one time. By their removal is meant their negation and non-existence. Examples: A- Like length and whiteness: they are contraries, and the relation between them is...
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the ninth relation: al-takhaluf (contrariety). Contrariety is a relation between two meanings where they can both be present in the same subject at the same time, and they can both be absent from the same subject at the same time. In other words, they are logically independent in terms of co-occurrence. The author gives one example: length and whiteness. These two attributes can coexist (e.g., a long white object) and can both be absent (e.g., a short black object). Unlike contradiction, where one must be present and the other absent, contrariety allows for both to be present or both absent. The text cuts off after the example, but the concept is clearly established.

Key Terms
التخالف (al-takhaluf): A relation between two meanings such that they can coexist in the same subject at the same time and can both be absent from that subject at the same time. [Note: In some logic texts, takhaluf is distinguished from tadaad (contradiction) where one must be present; here it is defined as possible coexistence and possible absence.]

B) Whiteness and blackness
C) Length and whiteness
D) Knowledge and ignorance

Key Takeaway
Contrariety (al-takhaluf) is defined by the possibility of both meanings being present together or both being absent together, with unity of time and place.

The Purpose of Definitions and the Explanatory Statement
Author introduces the section on definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dual purpose of a definition (mu'arraf) and distinguish between essential and descriptive definitions.

Core Idea
Definitions either reveal an essence or distinguish via non-essential marks; both are valid.

In this opening to the section on definitions, the author connects definitions to the earlier topics of universals, signification, and relations. He reminds us that the path to a concept is called a mu'arraf or qawl sharh, and it serves two possible goals: either to produce a mental picture of the thing's quiddity (mahiyya) or to distinguish it from others using its specific properties. This lesson unpacks that dual purpose and the intuitive nature of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms: mu'arraf and qawl sharh
Locate where the author defines these terms. Notice that mu'arraf is described as the path to conception, and qawl sharh as the explanatory statement that clarifies a simple term.
The Arabic phrase 'al-tariq al-kalami al-muwassil ila tasawwur shay'' is key.
2
Distinguish the two purposes
Read the sentences describing the goal: 'obtaining its picture in the mind' (hasul suratihi fi al-dhihn) and 'distinguishing it from others' (tamyizuhu 'an ghayrihi). These correspond to essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm).
The author mentions 'maahiyya and dhatiyyat' for the first, and 'khasais' for the second.
3
Recognize the intuitive basis
Note the phrase 'we know by intuition' (nadrik bi al-badaha). The author asserts that the necessity of conditions for definition is self-evident.
This prepares the reader for the formal rules to come.
4
Relate to Islamic sciences
Consider how definitions are used in fiqh (e.g., defining salah, zakah) or kalam (defining 'ilm, qadim). The two purposes correspond to hadd haqiqi and rasm.
For example, the definition of 'salah' as 'specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' is a rasm, not a hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹) المعرّفات
«القول الشارح»
في هذا المبحث تنكشف لنا مقاصد التصورات التي سبق أن عرفنا مبادئها في مباحث الكلي والجزئي، والكليات الخمس، والدلالات وأقسامها، والنسب وأقسامها.
وقد سبق أن عرفنا أن الطريق الكلامي الموصل إلى تصور شيء من الأشياء أو معنى من المعاني يسمى (معرّفاً) ويسمى أيضاً (قولاً شارحاً) أي: قولاً شارحاً للمفرد المطلوب حصول صورته في الذهن، أو تمييزه عن غيره.
وعرفنا أيضاً أن للمعرّف (القول الشارح) ضوابط لا بد من التزامها فيه حتى يكون سليماً موصلاً إلى تصور صحيح أو تمييز واضح.
وقد آن لنا أن نبحث في المعرّفات وأقسامها وضوابطها، وفيما يلي بيان ذلك.
قد نطرح على مخاطبنا مفرداً من المفردات التصورية، وقد يكون هذا المخاطب جاهلاً بصورة هذا المفرد، فنجد أنفسنا ملزمين بتعريفه به، حتى يتصوره على حقيقته ويدرك ماهيته وذاتياته، أو حتى يميزه عن غيره بصفات هي من خصائصه ولو لم تكن داخلة في عناصره الذاتية، وطريقنا لبلوغ هذه الغاية يكون عن طريق الكلام الذي يشرح المفرد ليتصوره المخاطب تصوراً صحيحاً يميزه عما سواه.
وندرك بالبداهة أنه لا بد لهذا القول الشارح (المعرّف) الذي نشرح به
(9) Definitions [al-Mu'arrafat] 'The Explanatory Statement' In this section, the aims of concepts that we have previously known their principles in the discussions of the universal and particular, the five universals, significations and their divisions, and relations and their divisions, are revealed to us. We have already known that the discursive path leading to the conception of something or a meaning is called (mu'arraf) and is also called (qawl sharh), i.e., an explanatory statement for the simple term that is desired to have its picture obtained in the mind, or to distinguish it from others. We also know that the definition (the explanatory statement) has rules that must be adhered to so that it is sound, leading to a correct conception or clear distinction. Now it is time for us to examine the definitions, their divisions, and their rules, and the following is an explanation of that. We may present to our interlocutor a simple term from conceptual simples, and this interlocutor may be ignorant of the picture of this simple term, so we find ourselves obliged to define it to him, so that he may conceive it in its reality and apprehend its quiddity and essential attributes, or distinguish it from others by attributes that are its characteristics even if they are not included in its essential elements. And our path to achieving this goal is through speech that explains the simple term so that the interlocutor conceives it correctly distinguishing it from what is other than it. And we know by intuition that this explanatory statement (the definition) by which we explain...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a new section on definitions (al-mu'arrafat). The author first ties this section to previous chapters: discussions on universal/particular, the five universals, signification, and relations. He then reminds the reader that the verbal path leading to a concept (tasawwur) is called a mu'arraf (definition) or qawl sharh (explanatory statement). Its purpose is either to produce a mental picture (sura) of a simple term or to distinguish it from others. The author emphasizes that definitions have conditions (dawabit) that must be met to be sound, leading to correct conception or clear distinction. The passage sets the stage by explaining the practical situation: when an interlocutor is ignorant of a concept, we are obliged to define it. The definition can be either a real definition—revealing the essence (mahiyya) and essential attributes (dhatiyyat)—or a descriptive definition—distinguishing by properties (khasais) that are not essential but specific. The author notes that this explanation is intuitive (bi al-badaha), preparing to detail the rules and types of definitions in what follows.

A) To produce a mental picture or to distinguish from others
B) To prove or to refute
C) To divide or to classify
D) To affirm or to negate

Key Takeaway
A definition (mu'arraf) is either a real definition giving the essence or a descriptive definition giving distinguishing properties; both are valid as long as they lead to correct conception.

Why a Definition Must Be Exactly Coextensive with the Definiendum
Author states and illustrates the requirement that a definition must be coextensive with the definiendum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given definition is coextensive (musāwī) with the term it defines, and explain why broader or narrower definitions fail.

Core Idea
A valid definition must include exactly the same individuals as the term defined—no more, no less.

In this lesson, we examine the first and most fundamental condition of a correct definition: equality of extension. The author emphasises that a definition must apply to precisely the same things as the definiendum, whether universal or particular. Understanding this condition is essential for sound conceptualisation in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the defining expression
Find the term being defined (the definiendum) and the words used to define it (the definition). For example, in 'human is an animal', the definiendum is 'human' and the definition is 'animal'.
2
Compare their extensions
List the individuals or instances that fall under the definiendum and those that fall under the definition. Check if they are the same set. For 'human is animal', 'animal' includes horses, snakes, etc.—so its extension is larger than 'human'.
3
Check for equality
If the sets are identical, the definition is coextensive (musāwī). If the definition applies to more (broader), less (narrower), or completely different (mubāyin) individuals, it violates the condition.
The author lists three violations: a‘amm (broader), akhaṣṣ (narrower), mubāyin (different).
4
Conclude about the definition's validity
Only a coextensive definition is valid. A broader definition includes extraneous items; a narrower one excludes genuine ones; a different one replaces the object entirely.
This condition is foundational for all later discussions on definition in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
«القول الشارح»
في هذا المبحث تنكشف لنا مقاصد التصورات التي سبق أن عرفنا مبادئها في مباحث الكلي والجزئي، والكليات الخمس، والدلالات وأقسامها، والنسب وأقسامها.
وقد سبق أن عرفنا أن الطريق الكلامي الموصل إلى تصور شيء من الأشياء أو معنى من المعاني يسمى (معرّفاً) ويسمى أيضاً (قولاً شارحاً) أي: قولاً شارحاً للمفرد المطلوب حصول صورته في الذهن، أو تمييزه عن غيره.
وعرفنا أيضاً أن للمعرّف (القول الشارح) ضوابط لا بد من التزامها فيه حتى يكون سليماً موصلاً إلى تصور صحيح أو تمييز واضح.
