Definition and Division of Inference (Istidlal)
Author defines istidlal and divides it into direct and indirect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istidlal and distinguish between direct and indirect inference using examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Inference is the bridge from known propositions to new knowledge, divided into direct and indirect types.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of inference (istidlal) in Islamic logic. The author provides a clear definition, illustrates it with three key theological arguments, and then presents the major division into direct and indirect inference. Understanding this distinction is critical for evaluating how proofs are constructed in kalam, fiqh, and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the dual definition
Read the two equivalent definitions: inferring an unknown proposition from known ones, or arriving at an unknown judgment of assent by observing known judgments. Note the emphasis on rationality and novelty of the conclusion.
The second definition uses the term 'hukm tasdiqi' (judgment of assent).
2
Analyze the three examples
Examine each example: (A) using contradiction to prove the First Being's existence, (B) syllogism for the world's origination, (C) conditional argument for God's oneness. Identify the premises and conclusion in each.
Example B is a categorical syllogism; Example C is a conditional (modus tollens).
3
Identify the division into direct and indirect
Note that direct inference uses only one premise via opposition, conversion, or conditional implication. Indirect inference uses multiple premises and includes syllogism, induction, and analogy.
Direct inference is also called immediate inference (istidlal mubashir).
4
Distinguish subcategories
Direct inference operates through three mechanisms: opposition (taqabul), conversion (‘aks), and implication of conditionals (talazum al-shartiyyat). Indirect inference includes qiyas (syllogism), istiqra’ (induction), and tamthil (analogy).
These subcategories will be detailed in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال: هو استنتاج قضية مجهولة من قضية أو من عدة قضايا معلومة. أو: هو التوصل إلى حكم تصديقي مجهول بملاحظة حكم تصديقي معلوم، أو بملاحظة حكمين فأكثر من الأحكام التصديقية المعلومة. فهو إذاً عملية عقلية منطقية ينتقل فيها الباحث من قضية أو عدة قضايا إلى قضية أخرى تستخلص منها مباشرة دون اللجوء إلى التجربة. والأصل في القضايا المستنتجة أن تكون جديدة بالنسبة إلى القضايا التي دلّت عليها، وإلا لم يكن معنى للاستدلال، ولكن قد تكون غير ذلك إذا كانت طريقاً لإقامة الحجة على الغير. أمثلة: أ ـ كالتوصل إلى إثبات النقيض بملاحظة كون نقيضه منفياً، فمن عرف استحالة كون العدم المطلق هو الأصل بالنسبة إلى الموجود الأول الذي صدرت عنه الكائنات؛ عرف لزوماً بموجب تقابل النقيضين وجوب كون وجوده هو الأصل، وأن وجوده لم يكن مسبوقاً بعدم، لأن ادّعاء السبق بالعدم يفضي إلى محال، ولما سقط هذا النقيض ثبت نقيضه حتماً. ب ـ وكالتوصل إلى معرفة حدوث العالم بملاحظة القضيتين التاليتين: العالم متغير ـ وكل متغير حادث ـ إذن فالعالم حادث. جـ ـ وكالتوصل إلى معرفة أن الله واحدٌ لا شريك له بملاحظة القضيتين التاليتين : لو كان في السماء والأرض آلهة بحقٍّ غير الله لفسدتا (ببرهان العقل) لكنهما لم تفسدا (ببرهان المشاهدة)، فينتج أنه لا توجد آلهة متعدّدة معبودة بحقّ . ووجود الخالق في الأصل مسلم به إلّا أن الدليل هنا لنفي التعدد. والاستدلال ينقسم إلى قسمين : القسم الأول : الاستدلال المباشر، وهو الذي لا يحتاج فيه الباحث إلى أكثر من قضية واحدة . وهذا يكون فيما يسمى بأحكام القضايا (التقابل ـ العكس ـ تلازم الشرطيات) . ويتم بهذا القسم الاستدلال بصدق قضية على صدق قضية أخرى أو كذبها . أو الاستدلال بكذب قضية على صدق قضية أخرى أو كذبها . وذلك بمقتضى التقابل العقلي، أو بمقتضى أحكام عكس القضية التي يستلزمها هذا العكس، أو بمقتضى التلازم العقلي الذي يكون في القضايا الشرطية . وسيأتي بيانها في مواضعها . القسم الثاني : الاستدلال غير المباشر، وهو الذي يحتاج فيه الباحث إلى أكثر من قضية واحدة حتى يتوصل إلى النتيجة المطلوبة . وهذا يكون في الأدلة (الحجج) التالية : (القياس ـ الاستقراء ـ التمثيل) . ولكل من هذه الأدلة (الحجج) بحث خاصّ به .
Inference: It is deducing an unknown proposition from one or several known propositions. Or: It is arriving at an unknown judgment of assent by observing a known judgment of assent, or by observing two or more known judgments of assent. So it is a rational logical process in which the researcher moves from one or several propositions to another proposition extracted directly from them without resorting to experience. The original condition of the deduced propositions is that they be new relative to the propositions that indicated them, otherwise there would be no meaning to inference, but it may be otherwise if it is a method of establishing proof against another. Examples: A — Like arriving at proof of the contradictory by observing that its contradictory is negated. So whoever knows the impossibility of absolute non-existence being the origin with respect to the First Being from which all things originated; necessarily knows, by virtue of the opposition of contradictories, the necessity of His existence being the origin, and that His existence was not preceded by non-existence, because claiming precedence by non-existence leads to an absurdity, and when this contradictory falls, its opposite is necessarily established. B — And like arriving at knowledge of the temporal origination of the world by observing the following two propositions: The world is changing — and everything changing is temporally originated — therefore the world is temporally originated. C — And like arriving at knowledge that God is One without partner by observing the following two propositions: If there were gods in the heavens and the earth rightfully other than God, they would both have corrupted (by rational proof) but they have not corrupted (by observational proof), so it results that there are no multiple gods worshiped rightfully. And the existence of the Creator is originally accepted, but the proof here is to negate multiplicity. And inference is divided into two parts: The first part: direct inference, which does not require the researcher more than one proposition. This occurs in what is called the rulings of propositions (opposition, conversion, implication of conditionals). And by this part inference is made from the truth of a proposition to the truth or falsehood of another proposition, or inference from the falsehood of a proposition to the truth or falsehood of another proposition. That is according to rational opposition, or according to the rulings of the converse of the proposition required by this conversion, or according to rational implication found in conditional propositions. And its explanation will come in its places. The second part: indirect inference, which requires the researcher more than one proposition to arrive at the desired result. This occurs in the following proofs (arguments): (syllogism, induction, analogy). And each of these proofs (arguments) has its own discussion.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines inference (istidlal) and classifies it. The author begins by giving two equivalent definitions: inference is deducing an unknown proposition from one or more known propositions, or arriving at an unknown judgment of assent by observing one or more known judgments of assent. Emphasis is placed on the logical and rational nature of the process, and that the conclusion should ideally be new knowledge, though sometimes inference may be used to present proof to others even if the conclusion is known. Three examples follow: the first uses the principle of contradiction to prove that the First Being's existence is primordial; the second is a categorical syllogism proving the world is temporally originated; the third is a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) proving the oneness of God. Then inference is divided into two types: direct inference (istidlal mubashir), which uses only one proposition and relies on opposition, conversion, or implication of conditionals; and indirect inference (istidlal ghayr mubashir), which uses more than one proposition and consists of syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). The passage sets the stage for detailed discussions to come.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
قضية
الاستدلال المباشر
الاستدلال غير المباشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of indirect inference (الاستدلال غير المباشر)?
A) Inferring the truth of a proposition from the falsehood of its contradictory by opposition.
B) The argument: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.'
C) Using the conversion of a proposition to infer another.
D) Inferring from the truth of a conditional to the truth of its consequent.

Key Takeaway
Inference is the rational process of moving from known to unknown propositions, and its division into direct and indirect types is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Definition and Types of Induction (al-Istiqra')
Author defines induction and its types with examples from experimental science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define induction, distinguish complete from incomplete induction, and identify the role of experiment in the inductive process.

Core Idea
Induction traces particulars to a general judgment; completeness determines certainty.

The author introduces induction as a method of non-direct inference, involving both intellectual and sensory processes. He defines it formally and illustrates with experiments. You will learn to differentiate complete induction (all particulars) from incomplete induction (some particulars).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of induction
The author gives two equivalent definitions: 'tracing all or some particulars to reach a general judgment' and 'transfer of thought from a judgment on the particular to a judgment on the universal.' Locate both in the passage.
The first definition emphasizes coverage; the second emphasizes logical movement.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'tracing' (tataabu') or 'transfer' (intiqal). The differentia includes 'all or some particulars' and 'general judgment covering them.' This is a real definition (hadd haqiqi).
3
Distinguish complete vs incomplete induction
Complete induction examines all particulars in a field; incomplete examines only some. The author uses the example of testing all metals for heat effect vs. testing only some.
Completeness is necessary but not sufficient for certainty; conditions of certainty also matter.
4
Understand the role of experiment
Experiment (tajriba) is the method of posing practical questions to things to observe responses. The author distinguishes intended experiments (deliberate) from unintended ones (accidental). Both provide data for induction.
Experiments in Islamic science are premised on nature being subjugated by God, making consistent observation meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من طرق الاستدلال غير المباشر طريقة الاستقراء. والاستقراء عملية فكرية وحسية معاً، وقد تستخدم هذه العملية التجربة المقصودة إضافة إلى التجارب غير المقصودة التي تمر في حياة الإنسان.
والتجربة المقصودة هي بمثابة طرح الأسئلة العملية على الأشياء، لمعرفة مدى استجابتها، أو عدم استجابتها، أو مدى تأثرها بما طرح عليها، وملاحظة كل ذلك وتقييده، باعتباره نتائج قدمتها التجارب.
أمثلة :
أ - نقول للمادة مثلاً : هل أنت قابلة للتأكسد بالحموض؟ فتقول المادة بلسان الحال: إنني مسخرة بين يديكم بتسخير الله، فجربوني مرات عديدة في ظروف شتى، وأحوال مختلفة، تأخذوا الجواب الصحيح من واقع حالي.
ب - ونقول للنحاس مثلاً : هل أنت صالح لأن نصنع منك بندقية وسائر أسلحة الحرب؟ .
فيقول النحاس : أنا مسخر بين يديكم بتسخير الله، فجربوني وامتحنوني بصور شتى، وأشكال عدة، وظروف مختلفات، أعطكم جواب أسئلتكم عملياً.
جـ - وإذا أردنا أن نعرف مدى تأثر المعادن بالحرارة فإننا نمرّ على جميع المعادن معدناً معدناً وهي تعطينا عن طريق التجربة أجوبتها، ونحن نقوم بتقييد هذه الأجوبة وتسجيلها، فإن نحن استقصينا كل المعادن، كان عملنا في هذا المجال استقراءً تاماً، وإلا كان عملنا استقراء ناقصاً.
ثم يأتي الفكر بعد عمليات الاستقراء فيحاول استنباط تفسير للظواهر التي توصل إليها وقيدها، وهذا التفسير يدور في فلك قوانين العلل والمعلولات، والأسباب والمسببات، ثم يستخلص ما ثبت لديه أو ما ترجح لديه من ضوابط وقواعد، أو قوانين كلية عامة، تتعلق بالمجال الذي تتبع جزئياته بعمليات الاستقراء.
تعريف الاستقراء:
فالاستقراء إذن هو تتبع الجزئيات كلها أو بعضها للوصول إلى حكم عام يشملها جميعاً.
أو هو انتقال الفكر من الحكم على الجزئي إلى الحكم على الكلي الذي يدخل الجزئي تحته.
قيمة ما نتوصل إليه بالاستقراء من معرفة:
وما نتوصل إليه بالاستقراء من معرفة له عدة أحوال:
١ - فإن توافرت فيه شروط اليقين كان علماً يقينياً، أي كان من الحقائق النهائية التي نتوصل إليها، والتي لا تقبل بحال من الأحوال احتمال النقض.
٢ - وإن لم يتوافر فيه شروط اليقين كان دون ذلك بحسب درجة قوته أو ضعفه.
أ ـ فإما أن يكون ظناً راجحاً على ما يخالفه، وهذا الظن الراجح
Among the methods of non-direct inference is the method of induction. Induction is an intellectual and sensory process together, and this process may use intended experiment in addition to unintended experiments that occur in human life. The intended experiment is like posing practical questions to things, to know the extent of their response, or lack of response, or the extent of their being affected by what is posed to them, and observing all that and recording it, considering it as results provided by experiments. Examples: A - We say to a substance, for example: Are you susceptible to oxidation by acids? So the substance says by the tongue of the situation: I am subjugated to you by the subjugation of God, so test me many times in various conditions and different circumstances, you will take the correct answer from my actual reality. B - And we say to copper, for example: Are you suitable for making a rifle and other weapons of war? So copper says: I am subjugated to you by the subjugation of God, so test me and examine me in various forms, several shapes, and different conditions, I will give you the answer to your questions practically. C - And if we want to know the extent of the effect of heat on metals, we go through all metals, metal by metal, and they give us their answers through experiment, and we record these answers and register them. If we survey all metals, our work in this field is complete induction; otherwise, our work is incomplete induction. Then thought comes after induction operations and tries to derive an interpretation of the phenomena it reached and recorded, and this interpretation revolves in the orbit of the laws of causes and effects, and reasons and their results, then it extracts what has been confirmed or what has been deemed probable from controls and rules, or general universal laws, related to the field whose particulars were traced by induction operations. Definition of induction: Induction is the tracing of all or some of the particulars to reach a general judgment that includes them all. Or it is the transfer of thought from a judgment on the particular to a judgment on the universal under which the particular falls. The value of the knowledge we attain through induction: What we attain through induction of knowledge has several states: 1 - If the conditions of certainty are met, it is certain knowledge, i.e., it is among the final truths we attain, which in no way accept the possibility of refutation. 2 - And if the conditions of certainty are not met, it is below that according to its degree of strength or weakness. a - It may be a strong opinion outweighing its opposite, and this strong opinion...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage defines induction (al-istiqra') as a method of non-direct inference. The author begins by describing induction as both an intellectual and sensory process, incorporating both intended and unintended experiments. Intended experiments are likened to asking practical questions to things to observe their responses. Three examples illustrate this: (a) testing a substance for oxidation, (b) testing copper for suitability in weapon-making, and (c) testing the effect of heat on metals by examining each metal individually. Here the author distinguishes complete induction (istiqra' tam) when all particulars are examined, and incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis) when only some are examined. After the experimental phase, the intellect interprets the phenomena using causal laws and extracts general rules. The formal definition is then given: induction is tracing all or some particulars to reach a general judgment, or moving from a judgment on a particular to a judgment on the universal that includes it. Finally, the epistemic value of inductive knowledge is classified: (1) when conditions of certainty are met, it yields certain knowledge that cannot be refuted; (2) if not, it is weaker, varying in strength—first mentioned is a strong opinion (zann rajih) that outweighs its opposite. The passage emphasizes that inductive knowledge can range from certainty to mere probability depending on completeness and conditions. All examples are from physical sciences, consistent with the Islamic principle that the natural world is subjugated by God and can be studied experimentally.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
الاستقراء التام
الاستقراء الناقص
التجربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the outcome when a jurist examines all known instances of a legal ruling (e.g., all types of intoxicants) to derive a general prohibition based on the 'illa of intoxication? (Assume certainty conditions are met.)
A) Strong opinion (zann rajih) that it is prohibited
B) Certain knowledge (yaqin) that all intoxicants are prohibited
C) Merely a guess (wahm)
D) Complete doubt (shakk)

Key Takeaway
Induction is defined as tracing all or some particulars to reach a general judgment; complete induction yields certainty, incomplete yields probability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider a jurist who examines three types of fermented drinks and concludes all are prohibited. Is this complete or incomplete induction? Why?
?
2. Using the definition from the lesson, identify the genus and differentia in a definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) from usul al-fiqh: 'Qiyas is the application of a known ruling to a new case due to shared 'illa.' Compare with induction.

Epistemic Value of Inductive Knowledge
Author classifies inductive knowledge into certain and probable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the outcomes of induction as certain knowledge (yaqin) or strong opinion (zann rajih) based on whether conditions of certainty are met.

Core Idea
Inductive knowledge ranges from absolute certainty to probable opinion depending on completeness and conditions.

After defining induction, the author discusses what kind of knowledge it produces. He outlines two main states: certain knowledge when conditions are fulfilled, and weaker knowledge otherwise, starting with strong opinion. This lesson focuses on that classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main states of inductive knowledge
The author mentions two states: certain knowledge (yaqin) when conditions of certainty are met, and knowledge below certainty (starting with strong opinion, zann rajih) when conditions are not met.
The passage only begins to list the weaker states, mentioning strong opinion first.
2
Determine conditions for certainty
The passage does not enumerate specific conditions but implies that completeness and consistency of observation are necessary. In Islamic logic, typical conditions include complete enumeration, consistent results, and absence of counterexamples.
The author refers to 'conditions of certainty' (shurut al-yaqin) without detailing them.
3
Distinguish certain knowledge from strong opinion
Certain knowledge is final and cannot be refuted; strong opinion outweighs its opposite but is still probable. The passage states that yaqin does not admit the possibility of refutation, whereas weaker knowledge varies in strength.
4
Apply the classification to a real inductive inference from fiqh
Consider the induction that all intoxicants are prohibited. If a jurist examines every known intoxicant and finds consistent prohibition due to intoxication, with no counterexample, and certainty conditions are met, the ruling is yaqin. If only a few are examined, it is zann rajih.
This application shows how the classification guides legal epistemology.

B - And we say to copper, for example: Are you suitable for making a rifle and other weapons of war? First Condition of a Scientific Hypothesis
Author states the first condition: hypothesis must be based on prior observation or experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first condition that a scientific hypothesis must satisfy according to this passage, and explain why it is necessary.

Core Idea
A scientific hypothesis must be grounded in observation, not mere imagination.

In this passage, the author begins to lay down the essential conditions (shurut) that distinguish a scientific hypothesis from mere speculation. He emphasizes that without such constraints, the endless mental probabilities would undermine the pursuit of knowledge. The first condition he presents is that any hypothesis must be preceded by pure observation or experiment, so that it offers a grounded explanation of a phenomenon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The author warns that not every imagined idea is suitable as a scientific hypothesis. Mental probabilities are endless and need constraints. Why is this a problem for knowledge?
This sets the stage for why conditions are needed.
2
Locate the first condition
Find the phrase 'أولاً : يجب أن يكون الفرض...' and note exactly what it requires: the hypothesis must be preceded by pure observation or experiment.
The condition is about prior empirical grounding.
3
Understand the requirement
The hypothesis should inspire an explanation of a phenomenon by attempting to state its cause or effect. This means the hypothesis is not arbitrary but derived from what is observed.
It links hypothesis formation to empirical data.
4
Explain the necessity
Why must the hypothesis be preceded by observation? Because without this, the field is open to infinite, unconstrained possibilities that would harm the cause of knowledge.
Empirical grounding gives the hypothesis scientific value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروط الفرض العلمي : من الواضح بالبديهة أنه لا يصلح أي فرض يطرحه التخيل لأن يكون فرضاً علمياً، فالاحتمالات الذهنية لا نهاية لها، وفتح مجالها بغير ضوابط ولا شروط لا يخدم قضية المعرفة، بل قد يسيء إليها إساءات بالغات، لذلك كان لا بد للفرض العلمي من وضع ضوابط له . أولاً : يجب أن يكون الفرض الذي يطرح على سبيل الاحتمال الظني مسبوقاً بملاحظة مجردة، أو تجربة وملاحظة، ليستلهم من ذلك تفسيراً للظاهرة، بمحاولة بيان سببها أو علتها، أو تفسيراً لما ينتج عنها، بمحاولة بيان
Conditions of the scientific hypothesis: It is obvious by necessity that not any hypothesis put forward by imagination is suitable to be a scientific hypothesis. Mental probabilities are endless, and opening their field without controls and conditions does not serve the cause of knowledge; rather, it may harm it severely. Therefore, it was necessary to set controls for the scientific hypothesis. First: The hypothesis that is put forward on a basis of probabilistic conjecture must be preceded by pure observation, or experiment and observation, so that from that he may derive an explanation of the phenomenon, by attempting to state its cause or 'illa, or an explanation of what results from it, by attempting to state...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage introduces the conditions (shurut) that a scientific hypothesis must satisfy. The author begins by emphasizing that not every imagined idea qualifies as a scientific hypothesis; without constraints, the field of infinite mental probabilities would harm knowledge. Hence, the first condition: the hypothesis must be preceded by observation or experiment. The researcher should use observation to infer the cause or effect of the phenomenon. This ensures the hypothesis is grounded in empirical reality, not pure speculation.

Key Terms

الاحتمال الظني

Key Takeaway
The first condition for a scientific hypothesis is that it must be grounded in prior observation or experiment, not mere imagination.

Method of Agreement: Critique and Application to Riba
Critique of the method of agreement and its application to the riba hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the method of agreement (tard) for extracting the 'illa in usul al-fiqh and apply it to a real hadith, recognizing its probabilistic nature.

Core Idea
The method of agreement cannot yield certainty; it only provides probable knowledge, as shown by the riba hadith debate.

The method of agreement (tard) is one way jurists extract the effective cause ('illa) of a legal ruling. However, this passage warns that relying on shared attributes across cases can lead to probabilistic conclusions, not certainty. By examining the famous riba hadith, you will see how scholars disagreed on the 'illa—some identifying weight, others edibility—precisely because the method cannot guarantee a unique causal link.

Guided Path
1
Understand the critique of tard
Read the first two paragraphs of the passage to identify two main criticisms: (1) it is nearly impossible to find cases that agree in only one circumstance due to natural complexity, and (2) any apparent agreement may be coincidental, not causal.
These critiques undermine the method's claim to certainty.
2
Examine the riba hadith
Read the hadith about six commodities (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt) and note the universal ruling: like for like, hand to hand, with prohibition of increase.
This hadith is the test case for the method.
3
Trace the scholars' application of tard
Identify the two groups of scholars: one group took all six as weighed commodities and deduced weight as 'illa; the other split them into gold/silver (currency/weight) and the rest (edible/measurable), deducing different 'illas.
Observe how different common attributes are extracted despite the same cases.
4
Assess the probabilistic outcome
Recognize that the passage states this method yields only probability (rajahan zanniyan). The divergence among scholars confirms the inconclusive nature of tard.
This exercise reinforces the need for other methods (e.g., co-presence with or without difference) to strengthen the causal inference.

وقد انتقدت هذه الطريقة بأنها صعبة التطبيق، إذ لا تكاد تتفق حالتان أو أكثر لظاهرة من الظواهر في ظرف واحد، مع الاختلاف في جميع الظروف الأخرى، إذ الطبيعة العامة معقدة، فهي تحتوي على كثير من الأسباب والمسببات المتشابكة المتداخلة، وبذلك تشتبه الأمور، ويكون من الصعب جداً فصل ظرف خاص عن بقية الظروف الأخرى.
وانتقدت باحتمال كون اتفاق الحالتين للظاهرة في ظرف واحد فقط واختلافهما فيما عداه، إنما كان من قبيل المصادفة المحضة، دون أن يكون هذا الظرف الواحد هو السبب للظاهرة أو مشتملاً على السبب، وهذا الانتقاد يؤيد أن هذه الطريقة لا تفيد يقيناً، وإنما تفيد رجحاناً ظنياً فقط.
هذه الطريقة هي إحدى طرق استخراج علة الحكم في الفقه الإسلامي.
نظر الأصوليون في قول الرسول ﷺ الذي رواه مسلم عن عبادة بن الصامت: «الذهب بالذهب، والفضة بالفضة، والبر بالبر، والشعير بالشعير، والتمر بالتمر، والملح بالملح، مثلاً بمثل، سواءً بسواء، يداً بيد، فإذا اختلفت هذه الأصناف فبيعوا كيف شئتم إذا كان يداً بيد» وفي رواية أبي سعيد الخدري بعد «يداً بيد» أن الرسول قال: «فمن زاد أو استزاد فقد أربى، والآخذ والمعطي فيه سواء».
وقد بحث المجتهدون في علة تحريم الربا في هذه الأصناف، فنظر بعضهم إليها جميعاً، فلم يجد صفة مشتركة بينها جميعاً إلا كونها موزونة، فحكم بأن علة تحريم الربا فيها كونها موزونة، فرأى على طريق القياس الأصولي أن كل عنصر موزون يتبايع فيه الناس يحرم فيه الربا، فإن اتحد الصنف وجب فيه التساوي (سواء بسواء) دون زيادة، ووجبت فيه المقابضة (يداً بيد) دون تأجيل وإن اختلف الصنفان لم يشترط فيهما التساوي، وإنما اشترط فيهما المقابضة.
ونظر بعضهم إليها فقسمها إلى قسمين:
فجعل الذهب والفضة قسماً، واستخرج الصفة المشتركة بينهما، وحكم بأنها هي علة الربا فيهما، وهذه الصفة هي النقدية، أو الوزن.
وجعل البر والشعير والتمر والملح قسماً ثانياً، واستخرج الصفة المشتركة بينها؛ وحكم بأنها هي علة الربا فيها، وهذه الصفة هي كونها مطعومة، فقاس عليها كل مطعوم قياساً أصولياً، أو كونها مكيلة فقاس عليها كل مكيل.
وتدخل هذه الطريقة ضمن أنواع «تخريج مناط الحكم» المعروفة في علم أصول الفقه.
واستخراج العلة بهذه الطريقة، استخراج لها عن طريق الطرد.
This method was criticized for being difficult to apply, since two or more cases of a phenomenon almost never agree in a single circumstance while differing in all other circumstances, for nature is complex, containing many intertwined and overlapping causes and effects, thus matters become confused, and it becomes very difficult to separate a specific circumstance from the rest of the other circumstances.
And it was criticized by the possibility that the agreement of two cases of the phenomenon in only one circumstance and their difference in others may be mere coincidence, without this single circumstance being the cause of the phenomenon or containing the cause. And this criticism supports that this method does not yield certainty, but only probability.
This method is one of the methods of extracting the cause (illa) of a ruling in Islamic jurisprudence.
The usul scholars considered the saying of the Messenger ﷺ narrated by Muslim from Ubadah ibn al-Samit: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver, wheat for wheat, barley for barley, dates for dates, salt for salt, like for like, equal for equal, hand to hand; if these types differ, then sell as you wish if it is hand to hand.' And in the narration of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri after 'hand to hand' that the Messenger said: 'Whoever increases or asks for an increase has committed riba, and the taker and the giver are equal therein.'
And the mujtahids investigated the cause of prohibition of riba in these types. Some looked at them all and found no common attribute among them except that they are weighed, so they ruled that the cause of prohibition of riba in them is that they are weighed. So they held, by way of legal analogy (qiyas usuli), that every weighed commodity that people trade is prohibited for riba. If the type is the same, equality (like for like) without increase is obligatory, and hand-to-hand exchange is obligatory without deferment. If the types differ, equality is not required, but hand-to-hand exchange is required.
And some looked at them and divided them into two parts:
They made gold and silver a part, and extracted the common attribute between them, and ruled that it is the cause of riba in them, and this attribute is being currency (naqdiyya) or weight.
And they made wheat, barley, dates, and salt a second part, and extracted the common attribute among them, and ruled that it is the cause of riba in them, and this attribute is being edible (mat'um), so they analogized every edible item by legal analogy, or being measurable, so they analogized every measured item.
And this method falls under the types of 'takhrij manat al-hukm' known in the science of usul al-fiqh.
And extracting the cause by this method is extraction by way of tard (concomitance).
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage critiques the method of agreement (tard) used to extract the 'illa (cause) of a legal ruling in Islamic jurisprudence. The author first points out two main criticisms: first, it is difficult to find two cases that agree in only one circumstance while differing in all others due to the complexity of nature; second, even if such agreement is found, it may be mere coincidence and does not yield certainty, only probability. Then the passage applies this method to the famous hadith on riba (usury) involving six commodities. The scholars differed: some viewed all six as weighed and thus took weight as the 'illa; others split them into two groups (gold/silver as currency, and the four food items as edible/measurable), extracting different 'illas for each. This shows how the method of agreement is used in practice to identify the common attribute across cases. The passage concludes that this method is called 'tard' (concomitance) and falls under 'takhrij manat al-hukm' in usul al-fiqh.

علة
الربا
تخريج مناط الحكم

Key Takeaway
The method of agreement (tard) is fallible and only yields probable knowledge; its application to the riba hadith shows differing conclusions, confirming its probabilistic nature.

The Method of Difference (Khalaf) for Extracting the 'Illa
Description of the method of difference as the inverse of the method of agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of difference to identify the cause (ʿilla) of a phenomenon in usul al-fiqh and other Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The method of difference finds the one circumstance that changes when the effect changes.

In the previous lesson, you learned the method of agreement, which looks for the common circumstance across differing cases. The method of difference is its logical inverse: it looks for the differing circumstance that correlates with the occurrence of the phenomenon. Both are essential tools for extracting the underlying cause in Islamic legal reasoning and other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify two cases
Select one case where the phenomenon (e.g., a ruling or effect) is present and another where it is absent. These cases must be as similar as possible in all other respects.
2
List all candidate circumstances
Enumerate all the circumstances (zuruf) present in the two cases. These are the potential causes of the phenomenon.
3
Compare and find the differing circumstance
Compare the two lists. Identify the one circumstance that is present when the phenomenon occurs but absent when it does not, while all other circumstances remain the same.
This is the 'unique differing circumstance'.
4
Conclude it is the cause
The differing circumstance is the cause (ʿilla) of the phenomenon according to the method of difference.
This method is also called 'tariqat al-khalaf' because it depends on a single difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - شرح الطريقة الثانية : وهي النظر في الاختلاف، أي النظر إلى حالات الظاهرة المدروسة، واستخراج الظرف الوحيد الذي تَخَلَّف وجود الظاهرة بتخلف وجوده، مع وجود الظروف الأخرى عند تخلف وجود الظاهرة .
يقول «ستيوارت ميل» في تحديد هذه الطريقة :
«إذا وجدت الظاهرة في حالة ولم توجد في حالة أخرى، وكانت الحالتان متفقتين في جميع الظروف إلا في ظرف واحد، يوجد عند وجود الظاهرة ولا يوجد عند اختفائها، كان هذا الظرف سبب الظاهرة» .
ليس خافياً أن هذه الطريقة هي عكس الطريقة الأولى، فالطريقة الأولى تعتمد على اختلاف الحالات في كل الظروف، إلا في الظرف الذي تحدث فيه الظاهرة فهو متفق، وهذه الطريقة الثانية تعتمد على اتفاق الحالات في كل الظروف إلا في الظرف الذي تحدث فيه الظاهرة، فهو مختلف .
مثال ذلك : مفرختان للدجاج متفقتان في كل ظروفهما إلا أن إحداهما معطاة نسبة ما من الرطوبة والأخرى جافة تماماً .
2 - Explanation of the second method: It is looking at difference, i.e., looking at the cases of the studied phenomenon, and extracting the unique circumstance that is absent when the phenomenon is absent, while other circumstances are present when the phenomenon is absent.
John Stuart Mill says in defining this method:
'If the phenomenon occurs in one case and does not occur in another case, and the two cases agree in all circumstances except one, which is present when the phenomenon occurs and absent when it is absent, then this circumstance is the cause of the phenomenon.'
It is not hidden that this method is the opposite of the first method. The first method relies on the cases differing in all circumstances except the circumstance in which the phenomenon occurs, which is common. This second method relies on the cases agreeing in all circumstances except the circumstance in which the phenomenon occurs, which is different.
Example: Two chicken incubators that agree in all their conditions except that one is given a certain amount of humidity and the other is completely dry.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the second method of extracting the cause, called the method of difference (khalaf). It begins by explaining that this method looks for the one circumstance whose absence coincides with the absence of the phenomenon, while all other circumstances remain present. The author quotes John Stuart Mill's formulation of the method. Then the passage contrasts this with the first method (agreement): in the first, cases differ in all circumstances except the one common circumstance; in the second, cases agree in all circumstances except the one that differs. The example given is of two chicken incubators that are identical except that one has humidity and the other is dry, implying that humidity is the cause of some phenomenon (e.g., hatching).

Key Terms

ظرف
سبب

D) By eliminating all circumstances that are not always present with the phenomenon.

Key Takeaway
The method of difference isolates a cause by comparing two nearly identical cases and identifying the single differing circumstance that correlates with the presence or absence of the phenomenon.

The Definition of Tamthil and Its Essential Condition
Author defines tamthil with two definitions and an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the essential condition of tamthil from both the logicians' and al-Ghazali's definitions, and apply it to an Islamic legal example.

Core Idea
Tamthil transfers a ruling only when a shared 'illa exists, not mere resemblance.

In this passage, two classical definitions of tamthil (analogy) are presented. Understanding them is vital because tamthil underlies qiyas in usul al-fiqh. Let's dissect these definitions to grasp what makes a valid analogy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Find where the logicians' definition ends and al-Ghazali's begins. Note they both describe tamthil as transferring a ruling from one particular to another.
2
Identify the key components
Both definitions involve a particular (juz'i), a ruling (hukm), and a transfer. The logicians explicitly mention 'participation in the cause of the ruling' (mushtaraka fi 'illat al-hukm).
3
Extract the essential condition
What is the non-negotiable element? The passage states: sharing the 'illa (cause). Without a shared 'illa, tamthil is invalid.
4
Compare the two definitions
Al-Ghazali's definition adds 'resemblance in some respect' but the logicians emphasize the 'illa. Note that the 'illa is the deeper reason.
Both definitions ultimately require a shared cause, though al-Ghazali frames it in terms of resemblance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف التمثيل:
وقد عرّف المناطقة التمثيل بقولهم:
التمثيل: قول مؤلف من قضايا تشتمل على بيان مشاركة جزئي لآخر في علة الحكم، فيثبت الحكم له.
وعرفه الغزالي بقوله:
«أن يوجد حكم في جزئي معين واحد، فينقل حكمه إلى جزئي آخر يشابهه بوجه ما».
أمثلة :
١ - الماء الكثير المستبحر سائل تجري فيه الفلك، فلو وجد زيت كثير مستبحر، أو لبن كثير مستبحر، لكان صالحاً لأن تجري الفلك فيه، نظراً إلى أن علة الجريان هي السيولة، وهي موجودة في الزيت واللبن كما هي موجودة في الماء.
Definition of Tamthil:
The logicians defined tamthil as follows:
Tamthil: a statement composed of propositions that include an explanation of the participation of one particular with another in the cause of a ruling, so the ruling is affirmed for it.
And al-Ghazali defined it as follows:
'A ruling exists in one specific particular, so its ruling is transferred to another particular that resembles it in some respect.'
Examples:
1. Vast water is a liquid in which ships sail. So if there were vast oil or vast milk, they would be suitable for ships to sail in them, considering that the cause of sailing is liquidity, which exists in oil and milk as it exists in water.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage defines tamthil (analogy) by presenting two classical definitions and an illustrative example. The first definition, from the logicians (al-mantiqiyyun), describes tamthil as a compound statement made up of premises that establish that one particular (juz'i) shares the 'illa (cause) of a ruling with another particular, thereby proving the same ruling for the second. The second definition, from al-Ghazali, emphasizes the transfer of a ruling from a known particular to another similar particular. The example demonstrates the structure: water is a liquid that allows sailing; oil and milk are also liquids; therefore, they also allow sailing. The 'illa is liquidity. The passage clarifies that tamthil operates by identifying a common cause that justifies extending the ruling across instances.

Key Terms
التمثيل
علة الحكم
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The essential condition for valid tamthil is the existence of a shared 'illa between the original and the new case.

Examples of Rationally Possible Existence
Author enumerates examples of rational possibility, distinguishing it from customary occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rationally possible events from merely customary occurrences using the criteria of intellectual non-contradiction and the condition of an adequate power.

Core Idea
Custom is not necessity: the intellect can conceive alternatives without contradiction.

Many events we consider impossible are only contrary to what we are used to. The author of this passage gives six vivid examples to show that rational possibility is much broader than empirical habit. By internalising this distinction, you will avoid conflating 'what usually happens' with 'what must happen'.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the concept of rational possibility
Recall that a rationally possible thing (mumkin) is one whose existence or non-existence the intellect can conceive without contradiction. The author emphasizes this by contrasting it with necessity (wajib) and impossibility (mustahil).
The key is that the intellect alone, without relying on observation, does not see a contradiction.
2
Examine the first example: fire burning
The author says fire is observed to burn, but the intellect finds no necessary connection between fire and burning. The intellect can conceive fire not burning or materials being incombustible. Therefore, 'fire burns' is rationally possible, not necessary.
This challenges the assumption that regularities in nature are logically forced.
3
Work through the remaining examples
Resurrection of the dead, instantaneous travel, lifting a mountain, and transformation of inanimate to animate are all said to be rationally possible. In each case, the intellect does not forbid it; the only requirement is an adequate power (quwwa mukāfi'a).
Notice that each example contrasts 'customary impossibility' with 'rational possibility'.
4
Identify the general principle
The passage concludes that every existent other than Allah is rationally possible in itself. The intellect does not necessitate its existence or non-existence. This principle is foundational in Islamic theology for discussing miracles, divine attributes, and contingency.
This distinguishes the Creator from all created things, which are inherently possible.
5
Apply the distinction to a new case
Consider a miracle from the Quran, such as the splitting of the moon. Customarily it never happened, but the intellect does not see a contradiction if an adequate power (divine power) is present. Thus it is rationally possible, not impossible.
This shows how the concept grounds the possibility of miracles without violating reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - النار محرقة، أمر مشاهد في الكون، فإذا تركنا العقل يفكر ويتأمل في العلاقة بين النار والإحراق، فإنه لا يرى أي ارتباط عقلي خاص بين الإحراق وبين النار، إلا أنه تكررت لديه في المشاهدة العادية للموجودات، مشاهدة أن النار تحرق، فأثبت لها هذه الصفة من المشاهدة، وأسند الأمر إلى أن المنظم لهذا الكون قد أعطاها هذه الصفة.
أما العقل بذاته، فلا يرى مانعاً عقلياً من أن تكون النار غير محرقة لو وجدت في الواقع كذلك، أو أن تكون المواد التي تلامسها النار فتحرقها غير قابلة للاحتراق، وذلك لأنه لا يوجد ارتباط عقلي بين النار وبين الإحراق.
إذن: فكون النار محرقة أمر ممكن في العقل وليس بواجب.
٣ - الأحياء التي نشاهدها إذا ماتت لا تعود إلى الحياة بعد موتها بحسب العادة المألوفة، لكن العقل لا يمنع من أن تعود الأجساد إلى الحياة بعد موتها، ولو أننا لم نشاهد بأعيننا ميتاً رجع حياً، جلّ ما في الأمر أن العقل يوجب لعودة الحياة وجود القوة المكافئة التي تتولى هذه الإعادة.
إذن: فالعودة إلى الحياة بعد الموت، أمرٌ ممكن عقلاً وليس بمستحيل.
٤ - اجتياز الإنسان المسافات البعيدة في أقطار الأرض أو السماء بطرفة عين أمر ممكن عقلاً، ولو أننا لا نستطيع أن نفعل ذلك بحسب العادة وضمن نظام الكون، لكن العقل لا يمنع من أن يحصل مثل هذا الاجتياز، إذا تهيأت الشروط الملائمة، ووجدت القوة المكافئة له.
إذن: فهو أمر ممكن عقلاً وليس بمستحيل.
٥ - رفع جبل كبير وتثبيته في الجوّ بين السماء والأرض أمر ممكن عقلاً، ولو أننا ننكر ذلك بحسب مجرى العادات، لكنه إذا تهيأت القوة المكافئة لرفع الجبل أمكن حدوث ذلك.
إذن: فرفع الجبل أمر ممكن عقلاً، وليس بمستحيل في حكم العقل.
٦ - انقلاب الجماد إلى حيوان أمرٌ ممكن عقلاً، ولو أننا بحسب العادة المستمرة لا نشاهد جمادات تنقلب إلى حيوانات، لكن العقل يحكم بأنه متى تهيأت الشروط الملائمة لهذا التحويل أمكن حصوله.
إذن: فهو أمر ممكن عقلاً وليس بمستحيل.
٧ - وهكذا كل موجود - سوى الله تعالى - فوجوده وصفاته، وكذلك انعدامه وانعدام صفاته، أمور ممكنة عقلاً، وليس شيء منها في حكم العقل المجرد بواجب ولا مستحيل.
2 - Fire is burning, an observed matter in the universe. If we leave the intellect to think and reflect on the relationship between fire and burning, it does not see any particular rational connection between burning and fire, except that it has repeated in its ordinary observation of existents the observation that fire burns, so it attributed this attribute to it from observation, and attributed the matter to the fact that the Organizer of this universe has given it this attribute. As for the intellect itself, it does not see any rational obstacle to fire being non-burning if it were found in reality as such, or that the materials that contact fire and are burned by it are not combustible, because there is no rational connection between fire and burning. Therefore: fire being burning is possible in the intellect, not necessary. 3 - The living things we observe, when they die, do not return to life after their death according to customary habit, but the intellect does not prevent bodies from returning to life after their death, even if we have not seen with our eyes a dead person returning alive. The most that can be said is that the intellect requires for the return of life the existence of an equivalent power that undertakes this return. Therefore: returning to life after death is a matter possible in the intellect, not impossible. 4 - A human traversing distant distances in the regions of the earth or sky in the blink of an eye is possible in the intellect, even if we cannot do that according to habit and within the system of the cosmos, but the intellect does not prevent such traversing from occurring if suitable conditions are prepared and the equivalent power exists. Therefore: it is a matter possible in the intellect, not impossible. 5 - Lifting a great mountain and fixing it in the air between heaven and earth is possible in the intellect, even if we deny that according to the course of habits, but if the equivalent power for lifting the mountain is prepared, its occurrence becomes possible. Therefore: lifting the mountain is a matter possible in the intellect, not impossible in the judgment of the intellect. 6 - The transformation of an inanimate object into an animal is possible in the intellect, even if according to continuous habit we do not observe inanimate objects transforming into animals, but the intellect judges that whenever the suitable conditions for this transformation are prepared, its occurrence is possible. Therefore: it is a matter possible in the intellect, not impossible. 7 - Thus every existent – except Allah the Exalted – its existence and attributes, and likewise its non-existence and the non-existence of its attributes, are matters possible in the intellect, and none of them is, in the judgment of the bare intellect, necessary or impossible.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the concept of rationally possible existence (al-mumkin al-wujud 'aqlan). The author begins by giving six concrete examples that are commonly considered impossible by custom but are in fact rationally possible. The first example is fire burning: although we observe fire always burning, the intellect finds no necessary connection between fire and burning; it is merely habitual. Similarly, resurrection of the dead, instantaneous travel over long distances, lifting a mountain, and transformation of inanimate objects into animals are all rationally possible because the intellect does not see any contradiction in them, even if they are contrary to customary experience. The author emphasizes that for each of these possibilities, the condition is the existence of an adequate power or suitable circumstances. The passage concludes with a general statement: every existent other than Allah is possible in itself, meaning that the intellect does not necessitate its existence or non-existence. This classification is foundational in Islamic logic and theology, distinguishing rational necessity from empirical habit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rational status of the resurrection of the dead?
A) It is impossible because we have never observed it.
B) It is possible because the intellect does not forbid it, though contrary to custom.
C) It is necessary because God has promised it.
D) It is doubtful because we lack evidence.

Key Takeaway
Rational possibility is defined by the intellect's ability to conceive something without contradiction, independent of customary observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is rationally impossible or merely contrary to custom: (a) A stone speaking, (b) A square circle, (c) Iron floating without support. For each, explain using the criteria from the passage.
?
Find a hadith that describes an event that is contrary to custom (e.g., the splitting of the moon, the night journey). Show how the concept of rational possibility, as taught in this lesson, supports its acceptance.
